OZ\xc3\x88ME\xe2\x80\x99S HOLIDAY                            403\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                           A Night in Acadie\r\n\r\n                           A Night in Acadie\r\n\r\n\r\nThere was nothing to do on the plantation so Tel\xc3\xa8sphore, having a few\r\ndollars in his pocket, thought he would go down and spend Sunday in the\r\nvicinity of Marksville.\r\n\r\nThere was really nothing more to do in the vicinity of Marksville than\r\nin the neighborhood of his own small farm; but Elvina would not be down\r\nthere, nor Amaranthe, nor any of Ma\xe2\x80\x99me Valtour\xe2\x80\x99s daughters to harass him\r\nwith doubt, to torture him with indecision, to turn his very soul into a\r\nweather-cock for love\xe2\x80\x99s fair winds to play with.\r\n\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore at twenty-eight had long felt the need of a wife. His home\r\nwithout one was like an empty temple in which there is no altar, no\r\noffering. So keenly did he realize the necessity that a dozen times at\r\nleast during the past year he had been on the point of proposing\r\nmarriage to almost as many different young women of the neighborhood.\r\nTherein lay the difficulty, the trouble which Tel\xc3\xa8sphore experienced in\r\nmaking up his mind. Elvina\xe2\x80\x99s eyes were beautiful and had often tempted\r\nhim to the verge of a declaration. But her skin was over swarthy for a\r\nwife; and her movements were slow and heavy; he doubted she had Indian\r\nblood, and we all know what Indian blood is for treachery. Amaranthe\r\npresented in her person none of these obstacles to matrimony. If her\r\neyes were not so handsome as Elvina\xe2\x80\x99s, her skin was fine, and being\r\nslender to a fault, she moved swiftly about her household affairs, or\r\nwhen she walked the country lanes in going to church or to the store.\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore had once reached the point of believing that Amaranthe would\r\nmake him an excellent wife. He had even started out one day with the\r\nintention of declaring himself, when, as the god of chance would have\r\nit, Ma\xe2\x80\x99me Valtour espied him passing in the road and enticed him to\r\nenter and partake of coffee and \xe2\x80\x9cbaign\xc3\xa9s.\xe2\x80\x9d He would have been a man of\r\nstone to have resisted, or to have remained insensible to the charms and\r\naccomplishments of the Valtour girls. Finally there was Ganache\xe2\x80\x99s widow,\r\nseductive rather than handsome, with a good bit of property in her own\r\nright. While Tel\xc3\xa8sphore was considering his chances of happiness or even\r\nsuccess with Ganache\xe2\x80\x99s widow, she married a younger man.\r\n\r\nFrom these embarrassing conditions, Tel\xc3\xa8sphore sometimes felt himself\r\nforced to escape; to change his environment for a day or two and thereby\r\ngain a few new insights by shifting his point of view.\r\n\r\nIt was Saturday morning that he decided to spend Sunday in the vicinity\r\nof Marksville, and the same afternoon found him waiting at the country\r\nstation for the south-bound train.\r\n\r\nHe was a robust young fellow with good, strong features and a somewhat\r\ndetermined expression--despite his vacillations in the choice of a wife.\r\nHe was dressed rather carefully in navy-blue \xe2\x80\x9cstore clothes\xe2\x80\x9d that fitted\r\nwell because anything would have fitted Tel\xc3\xa8sphore. He had been freshly\r\nshaved and trimmed and carried an umbrella. He wore\xe2\x80\x94a little tilted over\r\none eye\xe2\x80\x94a straw hat in preference to the conventional gray felt; for no\r\nother reason than that his uncle Tel\xc3\xa8sphore would have worn a felt, and\r\na battered one at that. His whole conduct of life had been planned on\r\nlines in direct contradistinction to those of his uncle Tel\xc3\xa8sphore, whom\r\nhe was thought in early youth to greatly resemble. The elder Tel\xc3\xa8sphore\r\ncould not read nor write, therefore the younger had made it the object\r\nof his existence to acquire these accomplishments. The uncle pursued the\r\navocations of hunting, fishing and moss-picking; employments which the\r\nnephew held in detestation. And as for carrying an umbrella, \xe2\x80\x9cNonc\xe2\x80\x9c\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore would have walked the length of the parish in a deluge before\r\nhe would have so much as thought of one. In short, Tel\xc3\xa8sphore, by\r\nadvisedly shaping his course in direct opposition to that of his uncle,\r\nmanaged to lead a rather orderly, industrious, and respectable\r\nexistence.\r\n\r\nIt was a little warm for April but the car was not uncomfortably crowded\r\nand Tel\xc3\xa8sphore was fortunate enough to secure the last available\r\nwindow-seat on the shady side. He was not too familiar with railway\r\ntravel, his expeditions being usually made on horse-back or in a buggy,\r\nand the short trip promised to interest him.\r\n\r\nThere was no one present whom he knew well enough to speak to: the\r\ndistrict attorney, whom he knew by sight, a French priest from\r\nNatchitoches and a few faces that were familiar only because they were\r\nnative.\r\n\r\nBut he did not greatly care to speak to anyone. There was a fair stand\r\nof cotton and corn in the fields and Tel\xc3\xa8sphore gathered satisfaction in\r\nsilent contemplation of the crops, comparing them with his own.\r\n\r\nIt was toward the close of his journey that a young girl boarded the\r\ntrain. There had been girls getting on and off at intervals and it was\r\nperhaps because of the bustle attending her arrival that this one\r\nattracted Tel\xc3\xa8sphore\xe2\x80\x99s attention.\r\n\r\nShe called good-bye to her father from the platform and waved good-bye\r\nto him through the dusty, sun-lit window pane after entering, for she\r\nwas compelled to seat herself on the sunny side. She seemed inwardly\r\nexcited and preoccupied save for the attention which she lavished upon a\r\nlarge parcel that she carried religiously and laid reverentially down\r\nupon the seat before her.\r\n\r\nShe was neither tall nor short, nor stout nor slender; nor was she\r\nbeautiful, nor was she plain. She wore a figured lawn, cut a little low\r\nin the back, that exposed a round, soft nuque with a few little clinging\r\ncirclets of soft, brown hair. Her hat was of white straw, cocked up on\r\nthe side with a bunch of pansies, and she wore gray lisle-thread gloves.\r\nThe girl seemed very warm and kept mopping her face. She vainly sought\r\nher fan, then she fanned herself with her handkerchief, and finally made\r\nan attempt to open the window. She might as well have tried to move the\r\nbanks of Red river.\r\n\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore had been unconsciously watching her the whole time and\r\nperceiving her straight he arose and went to her assistance. But the\r\nwindow could not be opened. When he had grown red in the face and wasted\r\nan amount of energy that would have driven the plow for a day, he\r\noffered her his seat on the shady side. She demurred\xe2\x80\x94there would be no\r\nroom for the bundle. He suggested that the bundle be left where it was\r\nand agreed to assist her in keeping an eye upon it. She accepted\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore\xe2\x80\x99s place at the shady window and he seated himself beside her.\r\n\r\nHe wondered if she would speak to him. He feared she might have mistaken\r\nhim for a Western drummer, in which event he knew that she would not;\r\nfor the women of the country caution their daughters against speaking to\r\nstrangers on the trains. But the girl was not one to mistake an Acadian\r\nfarmer for a Western traveling man. She was not born in Avoyelles parish\r\nfor nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 want anything to happen to it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all right w\xe2\x80\x99ere it is,\xe2\x80\x9d he assured her, following the direction of\r\nher glance, that was fastened upon the bundle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe las\xe2\x80\x99 time I came over to Foch\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s ball I got caught in the rain on\r\nmy way up to my cousin\xe2\x80\x99s house, an\xe2\x80\x99 my dress! J\xe2\x80\x99 vous r\xc3\xa9ponds! it was a\r\nsight. Li\xe2\x80\x99le mo\xe2\x80\x99, I would miss the ball. As it was, the dress looked\r\nlike I\xe2\x80\x99d wo\xe2\x80\x99 it weeks without doin\xe2\x80\x99-up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo fear of rain to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d he reassured her, glancing out at the sky,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut you can have my umbrella if it does rain; you jus\xe2\x80\x99 as well take it\r\nas not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no! I wrap\xe2\x80\x99 the dress roun\xe2\x80\x99 in toile-cir\xc3\xa9e this time. You goin\xe2\x80\x99 to\r\nFoch\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s ball? Didn\xe2\x80\x99 I meet you once yonda on Bayou Derbanne? Looks like\r\nI know yo\xe2\x80\x99 face. You mus\xe2\x80\x99 come f\xe2\x80\x99om Natchitoches pa\xe2\x80\x99ish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy cousins, the F\xc3\xa9deau family, live yonda. Me, I live on my own place\r\nin Rapides since \xe2\x80\x9992.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe wondered if she would follow up her inquiry relative to Foch\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s ball.\r\nIf she did, he was ready with an answer, for he had decided to go to the\r\nball. But her thoughts evidently wandered from the subject and were\r\noccupied with matters that did not concern him, for she turned away and\r\ngazed silently out of the window.\r\n\r\nIt was not a village; it was not even a hamlet at which they descended.\r\nThe station was set down upon the edge of a cotton field. Near at hand\r\nwas the post office and store; there was a section house; there were a\r\nfew cabins at wide intervals, and one in the distance the girl informed\r\nhim was the home of her cousin, Jules Trodon. There lay a good bit of\r\nroad before them and she did not hesitate to accept Tel\xc3\xa8sphore\xe2\x80\x99s offer\r\nto bear her bundle on the way.\r\n\r\nShe carried herself boldly and stepped out freely and easily, like a\r\nnegress. There was an absence of reserve in her manner; yet there was no\r\nlack of womanliness. She had the air of a young person accustomed to\r\ndecide for herself and for those about her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said yo\xe2\x80\x99 name was F\xc3\xa9deau?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, looking squarely at\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore. Her eyes were penetrating\xe2\x80\x94not sharply penetrating, but\r\nearnest and dark, and a little searching. He noticed that they were\r\nhandsome eyes; not so large as Elvina\xe2\x80\x99s, but finer in their expression.\r\nThey started to walk down the track before turning into the lane leading\r\nto Trodon\xe2\x80\x99s house. The sun was sinking and the air was fresh and\r\ninvigorating by contrast with the stifling atmosphere of the train.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said yo\xe2\x80\x99 name was F\xc3\xa9deau?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he returned. \xe2\x80\x9cMy name is Tel\xc3\xa8sphore Baquette.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 my name; it\xe2\x80\x99s Za\xc3\xafda Trodon. It looks like you ought to know me; I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99 know w\xe2\x80\x99y.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt looks that way to me, somehow,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied. They were satisfied to\r\nrecognize this feeling\xe2\x80\x94almost conviction\xe2\x80\x94of pre-acquaintance, without\r\ntrying to penetrate its cause.\r\n\r\nBy the time they reached Trodon\xe2\x80\x99s house he knew that she lived over on\r\nBayou de Glaize with her parents and a number of younger brothers and\r\nsisters. It was rather dull where they lived and she often came to lend\r\na hand when her cousin\xe2\x80\x99s wife got tangled in domestic complications; or,\r\nas she was doing now, when Foch\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s Saturday ball promised to be\r\nunusually important and brilliant. There would be people there even from\r\nMarksville, she thought; there were often gentlemen from Alexandria.\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore was as unreserved as she, and they appeared like old\r\nacquaintances when they reached Trodon\xe2\x80\x99s gate.\r\n\r\nTrodon\xe2\x80\x99s wife was standing on the gallery with a baby in her arms,\r\nwatching for Za\xc3\xafda; and four little bare-footed children were sitting in\r\na row on the step, also waiting; but terrified and struck motionless and\r\ndumb at sight of a stranger. He opened the gate for the girl but stayed\r\noutside himself. Za\xc3\xafda presented him formally to her cousin\xe2\x80\x99s wife, who\r\ninsisted upon his entering.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, b\xe2\x80\x99en, pour \xc3\xa7a! you got to come in. It\xe2\x80\x99s any sense you goin\xe2\x80\x99 to walk\r\nyonda to Foch\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s! Ti Jules, run call yo\xe2\x80\x99 pa.\xe2\x80\x9d As if Ti Jules could have\r\nrun or walked even, or moved a muscle!\r\n\r\nBut Tel\xc3\xa8sphore was firm. He drew forth his silver watch and looked at it\r\nin a business-like fashion. He always carried a watch; his uncle\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore always told the time by the sun, or by instinct, like an\r\nanimal. He was quite determined to walk on to Foch\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s, a couple of miles\r\naway, where he expected to secure supper and a lodging, as well as the\r\npleasing distraction of the ball.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I reckon I see you all to-night,\xe2\x80\x9d he uttered in cheerful\r\nanticipation as he moved away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll see Za\xc3\xafda; yes, an\xe2\x80\x99 Jules,\xe2\x80\x9d called out Trodon\xe2\x80\x99s wife\r\ngood-humoredly. \xe2\x80\x9cMe, I got no time to fool with balls, J\xe2\x80\x99 vous r\xc3\xa9ponds!\r\nwith all them chil\xe2\x80\x99ren.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s good-lookin\xe2\x80\x99; yes,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, when Tel\xc3\xa8sphore was out of\r\near-shot. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 dressed! it\xe2\x80\x99s like a prince. I didn\xe2\x80\x99 know you knew any\r\nBaquettes, you, Za\xc3\xafda.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s strange you don\xe2\x80\x99 know \xe2\x80\x99em yo\xe2\x80\x99 se\xe2\x80\x99f, cousine.\xe2\x80\x9d Well, there had been\r\nno question from Ma\xe2\x80\x99me Trodon, so why should there be an answer from\r\nZa\xc3\xafda?\r\n\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore wondered as he walked why he had not accepted the invitation\r\nto enter. He was not regretting it; he was simply wondering what could\r\nhave induced him to decline. For it surely would have been agreeable to\r\nsit there on the gallery waiting while Za\xc3\xafda prepared herself for the\r\ndance; to have partaken of supper with the family and afterward\r\naccompanied them to Foch\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s. The whole situation was so novel, and had\r\npresented itself so unexpectedly that Tel\xc3\xa8sphore wished in reality to\r\nbecome acquainted with it, accustomed to it. He wanted to view it from\r\nthis side and that in comparison with other, familiar situations. The\r\ngirl had impressed him\xe2\x80\x94affected him in some way; but in some new,\r\nunusual way, not as the others always had. He could not recall details\r\nof her personality as he could recall such details of Amaranthe or the\r\nValtours, of any of them. When Tel\xc3\xa8sphore tried to think of her he could\r\nnot think at all. He seemed to have absorbed her in some way and his\r\nbrain was not so occupied with her as his senses were. At that moment he\r\nwas looking forward to the ball; there was no doubt about that.\r\nAfterwards, he did not know what he would look forward to; he did not\r\ncare; afterward made no difference. If he had expected the crash of doom\r\nto come after the dance at Foch\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s, he would only have smiled in his\r\nthankfulness that it was not to come before.\r\n\r\n\r\nThere was the same scene every Saturday at Foch\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s! A scene to have\r\naroused the guardians of the peace in a locality where such commodities\r\nabound. And all on account of the mammoth pot of gumbo that bubbled,\r\nbubbled, bubbled out in the open air. Foch\xc3\xa9 in shirt-sleeves, fat, red\r\nand enraged, swore and reviled, and stormed at old black Dout\xc3\xa9 for her\r\nextravagance. He called her every kind of a name of every kind of animal\r\nthat suggested itself to his lurid imagination. And every fresh\r\ninvective that he fired at her she hurled it back at him while into the\r\npot went the chickens and the pans-full of minced ham, and the\r\nfists-full of onion and sage and piment rouge and piment vert. If he\r\nwanted her to cook for pigs he had only to say so. She knew how to cook\r\nfor pigs and she knew how to cook for people of les Avoyelles.\r\n\r\nThe gumbo smelled good, and Tel\xc3\xa8sphore would have liked a taste of it.\r\nDout\xc3\xa9 was dragging from the fire a stick of wood that Foch\xc3\xa9 had\r\nofficiously thrust beneath the simmering pot, and she muttered as she\r\nhurled it smouldering to one side:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVaux mieux y s\xe2\x80\x99m\xc3\xa9le ces affairs, lui; si non!\xe2\x80\x9d But she was all courtesy\r\nas she dipped a steaming plate for Tel\xc3\xa8sphore; though she assured him it\r\nwould not be fit for a Christian or a gentleman to taste till midnight.\r\n\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore having brushed, \xe2\x80\x9cspruced\xe2\x80\x9d and refreshed himself, strolled\r\nabout, taking a view of the surroundings. The house, big, bulky and\r\nweather-beaten, consisted chiefly of galleries in every stage of\r\ndecrepitude and dilapidation. There were a few chinaberry trees and a\r\nspreading live oak in the yard. Along the edge of the fence, a good\r\ndistance away, was a line of gnarled and distorted mulberry trees; and\r\nit was there, out in the road, that the people who came to the ball tied\r\ntheir ponies, their wagons and carts.\r\n\r\nDusk was beginning to fall and Tel\xc3\xa8sphore, looking out across the\r\nprairie, could see them coming from all directions. The little Creole\r\nponies galloping in a line looked like hobby horses in the faint\r\ndistance; the mule-carts were like toy wagons. Za\xc3\xafda might be among\r\nthose people approaching, flying, crawling ahead of the darkness that\r\nwas creeping out of the far wood. He hoped so, but he did not believe\r\nso; she would hardly have had time to dress.\r\n\r\nFoch\xc3\xa9 was noisily lighting lamps, with the assistance of an inoffensive\r\nmulatto boy whom he intended in the morning to butcher, to cut into\r\nsections, to pack and salt down in a barrel, like the Colfax woman did\r\nto her old husband\xe2\x80\x94a fitting destiny for so stupid a pig as the mulatto\r\nboy. The negro musicians had arrived: two fiddlers and an accordion\r\nplayer, and they were drinking whiskey from a black quart bottle which\r\nwas passed socially from one to the other. The musicians were really\r\nnever at their best till the quart bottle had been consumed.\r\n\r\nThe girls who came in wagons and on ponies from a distance wore, for the\r\nmost part, calico dresses and sun-bonnets. Their finery they brought\r\nalong in pillow-slips or pinned up in sheets and towels. With these they\r\nat once retired to an upper room; later to appear be-ribboned and\r\nbe-furbelowed; their faces masked with starch powder, but never a touch\r\nof rouge.\r\n\r\nMost of the guests had assembled when Za\xc3\xafda arrived\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cdashed up\xe2\x80\x9d would\r\nbetter express her coming\xe2\x80\x94in an open, two-seated buckboard, with her\r\ncousin Jules driving. He reined the pony suddenly and viciously before\r\nthe time-eaten front steps, in order to produce an impression upon those\r\nwho were gathered around. Most of the men had halted their vehicles\r\noutside and permitted their women folk to walk up from the mulberry\r\ntrees.\r\n\r\nBut the real, the stunning effect was produced when Za\xc3\xafda stepped upon\r\nthe gallery and threw aside her light shawl in the full glare of half a\r\ndozen kerosene lamps. She was white from head to foot\xe2\x80\x94literally, for her\r\nslippers even were white. No one would have believed, let alone\r\nsuspected that they were a pair of old black ones which she had covered\r\nwith pieces of her first communion sash. There is no describing her\r\ndress, it was fluffy, like a fresh powder-puff, and stood out. No wonder\r\nshe had handled it so reverentially! Her white fan was covered with\r\nspangles that she herself had sewed all over it; and in her belt and in\r\nher brown hair were thrust small sprays of orange blossom.\r\n\r\nTwo men leaning against the railing uttered long whistles expressive\r\nequally of wonder and admiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTiens! t\xe2\x80\x99es pareille comme ain mari\xc3\xa9e, Za\xc3\xafda;\xe2\x80\x9d cried out a lady with a\r\nbaby in her arms. Some young women tittered and Za\xc3\xafda fanned herself.\r\nThe women\xe2\x80\x99s voices were almost without exception shrill and piercing;\r\nthe men\xe2\x80\x99s, soft and low-pitched.\r\n\r\nThe girl turned to Tel\xc3\xa8sphore, as to an old and valued friend:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTiens! c\xe2\x80\x99est vous?\xe2\x80\x9d He had hesitated at first to approach, but at this\r\nfriendly sign of recognition he drew eagerly forward and held out his\r\nhand. The men looked at him suspiciously, inwardly resenting his stylish\r\nappearance, which they considered intrusive, offensive and demoralizing.\r\n\r\nHow Za\xc3\xafda\xe2\x80\x99s eyes sparkled now! What very pretty teeth Za\xc3\xafda had when she\r\nlaughed, and what a mouth! Her lips were a revelation, a promise;\r\nsomething to carry away and remember in the night and grow hungry\r\nthinking of next day. Strictly speaking, they may not have been quite\r\nall that; but in any event, that is the way Tel\xc3\xa8sphore thought about\r\nthem. He began to take account of her appearance: her nose, her eyes,\r\nher hair. And when she left him to go in and dance her first dance with\r\ncousin Jules, he leaned up against a post and thought of them: nose,\r\neyes, hair, ears, lips and round, soft throat.\r\n\r\nLater it was like Bedlam.\r\n\r\nThe musicians had warmed up and were scraping away indoors and calling\r\nthe figures. Feet were pounding through the dance; dust was flying. The\r\nwomen\xe2\x80\x99s voices were piped high and mingled discordantly, like the\r\nconfused, shrill clatter of waking birds, while the men laughed\r\nboisterously. But if some one had only thought of gagging Foch\xc3\xa9, there\r\nwould have been less noise. His good humor permeated everywhere, like an\r\natmosphere. He was louder than all the noise; he was more visible than\r\nthe dust. He called the young mulatto (destined for the knife) \xe2\x80\x9cmy boy\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand sent him flying hither and thither. He beamed upon Dout\xc3\xa9 as he\r\ntasted the gumbo and congratulated her: \xe2\x80\x9cC\xe2\x80\x99est toi qui s\xe2\x80\x99y connais, ma\r\nfille! \xe2\x80\x99cr\xc3\xa9 tonnerre!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore danced with Za\xc3\xafda and then he leaned out against the post;\r\nthen he danced with Za\xc3\xafda, and then he leaned against the post. The\r\nmothers of the other girls decided that he had the manners of a pig.\r\n\r\nIt was time to dance again with Za\xc3\xafda and he went in search of her. He\r\nwas carrying her shawl, which she had given him to hold.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99at time it is?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked him when he had found and secured her. They\r\nwere under one of the kerosene lamps on the front gallery and he drew\r\nforth his silver watch. She seemed to be still laboring under some\r\nsuppressed excitement that he had noticed before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s fo\xe2\x80\x99teen minutes pas\xe2\x80\x99 twelve,\xe2\x80\x9d he told her exactly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you\xe2\x80\x99d fine out w\xe2\x80\x99ere Jules is. Go look yonda in the card-room if\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s there, an\xe2\x80\x99 come tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d Jules had danced with all the prettiest\r\ngirls. She knew it was his custom after accomplishing this agreeable\r\nfeat, to retire to the card-room,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll wait yere till I come back?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll wait yere; you go on.\xe2\x80\x9d She waited but drew back a little into the\r\nshadow. Tel\xc3\xa8sphore lost no time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he\xe2\x80\x99s yonda playin\xe2\x80\x99 cards with Foch\xc3\xa9 an\xe2\x80\x99 some others I don\xe2\x80\x99 know,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe reported when he had discovered her in the shadow. There had been a\r\nspasm of alarm when he did not at once see her where he had left her\r\nunder the lamp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he look\xe2\x80\x94look like he\xe2\x80\x99s fixed yonda fo\xe2\x80\x99 good?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s got his coat off. Looks like he\xe2\x80\x99s fixed pretty comf\xe2\x80\x99table fo\xe2\x80\x99 the\r\nnex\xe2\x80\x99 hour or two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGi\xe2\x80\x99 me my shawl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou cole?\xe2\x80\x9d offering to put it around her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I ain\xe2\x80\x99t cole.\xe2\x80\x9d She drew the shawl about her shoulders and turned as\r\nif to leave him. But a sudden generous impulse seemed to move her, and\r\nshe added:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome along yonda with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey descended the few rickety steps that led down to the yard. He\r\nfollowed rather than accompanied her across the beaten and trampled\r\nsward. Those who saw them thought they had gone out to take the air. The\r\nbeams of light that slanted out from the house were fitful and\r\nuncertain, deepening the shadows. The embers under the empty gumbo-pot\r\nglared red in the darkness. There was a sound of quiet voices coming\r\nfrom under the trees.\r\n\r\nZa\xc3\xafda, closely accompanied by Tel\xc3\xa8sphore, went out where the vehicles\r\nand horses were fastened to the fence. She stepped carefully and held up\r\nher skirts as if dreading the least speck of dew or of dust.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnhitch Jules\xe2\x80\x99 ho\xe2\x80\x99se an\xe2\x80\x99 buggy there an\xe2\x80\x99 turn \xe2\x80\x99em \xe2\x80\x99roun\xe2\x80\x99 this way,\r\nplease.\xe2\x80\x9d He did as instructed, first backing the pony, then leading it\r\nout to where she stood in the half-made road.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou goin\xe2\x80\x99 home?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked her, \xe2\x80\x9cbetta let me water the pony.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeva mine.\xe2\x80\x9d She mounted and seating herself grasped the reins. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\r\naint goin\xe2\x80\x99 home,\xe2\x80\x9d she added. He, too, was holding the reins gathered in\r\none hand across the pony\xe2\x80\x99s back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99ere you goin\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeva you mine w\xe2\x80\x99ere I\xe2\x80\x99m goin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ain\xe2\x80\x99t goin\xe2\x80\x99 anyw\xe2\x80\x99ere this time o\xe2\x80\x99 night by yo\xe2\x80\x99se\xe2\x80\x99f?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99at you reckon I\xe2\x80\x99m \xe2\x80\x99fraid of?\xe2\x80\x9d she laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cTurn loose that ho\xe2\x80\x99se,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nat the same time urging the animal forward. The little brute started\r\naway with a bound and Tel\xc3\xa8sphore, also with a bound, sprang into the\r\nbuckboard and seated himself beside Za\xc3\xafda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ain\xe2\x80\x99t goin\xe2\x80\x99 anyw\xe2\x80\x99ere this time o\xe2\x80\x99 night by yo\xe2\x80\x99se\xe2\x80\x99f.\xe2\x80\x9d It was not a\r\nquestion now, but an assertion, and there was no denying it. There was\r\neven no disputing it, and Za\xc3\xafda recognizing the fact drove on in\r\nsilence.\r\n\r\nThere is no animal that moves so swiftly across a \xe2\x80\x99Cadian prairie as the\r\nlittle Creole pony. This one did not run nor trot; he seemed to reach\r\nout in galloping bounds. The buckboard creaked, bounced, jolted and\r\nswayed. Za\xc3\xafda clutched at her shawl while Tel\xc3\xa8sphore drew his straw hat\r\nfurther down over his right eye and offered to drive. But he did not\r\nknow the road and she would not let him. They had soon reached the\r\nwoods.\r\n\r\nIf there is any animal that can creep more slowly through a wooded road\r\nthan the little Creole pony, that animal has not yet been discovered in\r\nAcadie. This particular animal seemed to be appalled by the darkness of\r\nthe forest and filled with dejection. His head drooped and he lifted his\r\nfeet as if each hoof were weighted with a thousand pounds of lead. Any\r\none unacquainted with the peculiarities of the breed would sometimes\r\nhave fancied that he was standing still. But Za\xc3\xafda and Tel\xc3\xa8sphore knew\r\nbetter. Za\xc3\xafda uttered a deep sigh as she slackened her hold on the reins\r\nand Tel\xc3\xa8sphore, lifting his hat, let it swing from the back of his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow you don\xe2\x80\x99 ask me w\xe2\x80\x99ere I\xe2\x80\x99m goin\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d she said finally. These were the\r\nfirst words she had spoken since refusing his offer to drive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it don\xe2\x80\x99 make any diff\xe2\x80\x99ence w\xe2\x80\x99ere you goin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen if it don\xe2\x80\x99 make any diff\xe2\x80\x99ence w\xe2\x80\x99ere I\xe2\x80\x99m goin\xe2\x80\x99, I jus\xe2\x80\x99 as well tell\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d She hesitated, however. He seemed to have no curiosity and did not\r\nurge her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m goin\xe2\x80\x99 to get married,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nHe uttered some kind of an exclamation; it was nothing articulate\xe2\x80\x94more\r\nlike the tone of an animal that gets a sudden knife thrust. And now he\r\nfelt how dark the forest was. An instant before it had seemed a sweet,\r\nblack paradise; better than any heaven he had ever heard of.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99y can\xe2\x80\x99t you get married at home?\xe2\x80\x9d This was not the first thing that\r\noccurred to him to say, but this was the first thing he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, b\xe2\x80\x99en oui! with perfec\xe2\x80\x99 mules fo\xe2\x80\x99 a father an\xe2\x80\x99 mother! it\xe2\x80\x99s good\r\nenough to talk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99y couldn\xe2\x80\x99 he come an\xe2\x80\x99 get you? W\xe2\x80\x99at kine of a scound\xe2\x80\x99el is that to\r\nlet you go through the woods at night by yo\xe2\x80\x99se\xe2\x80\x99f?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou betta wait till you know who you talkin\xe2\x80\x99 about. He didn\xe2\x80\x99 come an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nget me because he knows I ain\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99fraid; an\xe2\x80\x99 because he\xe2\x80\x99s got too much\r\npride to ride in Jules Trodon\xe2\x80\x99s buckboard afta he done been put out o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nJules Trodon\xe2\x80\x99s house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99at\xe2\x80\x99s his name an\xe2\x80\x99 w\xe2\x80\x99ere you goin\xe2\x80\x99 to fine \xe2\x80\x99im?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYonda on the other side the woods up at ole Wat Gibson\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94a kine of\r\njustice the peace or something. Anyhow he\xe2\x80\x99s goin\xe2\x80\x99 to marry us. An\xe2\x80\x99 afta\r\nwe done married those t\xc3\xaates-de-mulets yonda on bayou de Glaize can say\r\nw\xe2\x80\x99at they want.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99at\xe2\x80\x99s his name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAndr\xc3\xa9 Pascal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe name meant nothing to Tel\xc3\xa8sphore. For all he knew, Andr\xc3\xa9 Pascal\r\nmight be one of the shining lights of Avoyelles; but he doubted it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou betta turn \xe2\x80\x99roun\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. It was an unselfish impulse that\r\nprompted the suggestion. It was the thought of this girl married to a\r\nman whom even Jules Trodon would not suffer to enter his house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI done give my word,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99at\xe2\x80\x99s the matta with \xe2\x80\x99im? W\xe2\x80\x99y don\xe2\x80\x99t yo\xe2\x80\x99 father and mother want you to\r\nmarry \xe2\x80\x99im?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99y? Because it\xe2\x80\x99s always the same tune! W\xe2\x80\x99en a man\xe2\x80\x99s down eve\xe2\x80\x99ybody\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngot stones to throw at \xe2\x80\x99im. They say he\xe2\x80\x99s lazy. A man that will walk\r\nfrom St. Landry plumb to Rapides lookin\xe2\x80\x99 fo\xe2\x80\x99 work; an\xe2\x80\x99 they call that\r\nlazy! Then, somebody\xe2\x80\x99s been spreadin\xe2\x80\x99 yonda on the Bayou that he drinks.\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99 b\xe2\x80\x99lieve it. I neva saw \xe2\x80\x99im drinkin\xe2\x80\x99, me. Anyway, he won\xe2\x80\x99t drink\r\nafta he\xe2\x80\x99s married to me; he\xe2\x80\x99s too fon\xe2\x80\x99 of me fo\xe2\x80\x99 that. He say he\xe2\x80\x99ll blow\r\nout his brains if I don\xe2\x80\x99 marry \xe2\x80\x99im.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon you betta turn roun\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I done give my word.\xe2\x80\x9d And they went creeping on through the woods\r\nin silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99at time is it?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked after an interval. He lit a match and\r\nlooked at his watch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s quarta to one. W\xe2\x80\x99at time did he say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tole \xe2\x80\x99im I\xe2\x80\x99d come about one o\xe2\x80\x99clock. I knew that was a good time to\r\nget away f\xe2\x80\x99om the ball.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe would have hurried a little but the pony could not be induced to do\r\nso. He dragged himself, seemingly ready at any moment to give up the\r\nbreath of life. But once out of the woods he made up for lost time. They\r\nwere on the open prairie again, and he fairly ripped the air; some\r\nflying demon must have changed skins with him.\r\n\r\nIt was a few minutes of one o\xe2\x80\x99clock when they drew up before Wat\r\nGibson\xe2\x80\x99s house. It was not much more than a rude shelter, and in the dim\r\nstarlight it seemed isolated, as if standing alone in the middle of the\r\nblack, far-reaching prairie. As they halted at the gate a dog within set\r\nup a furious barking; and an old negro who had been smoking his pipe at\r\nthat ghostly hour, advanced toward them from the shelter of the gallery.\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore descended and helped his companion to alight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe want to see Mr. Gibson,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke up Za\xc3\xafda. The old fellow had already\r\nopened the gate. There was no light in the house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarse Gibson, he yonda to ole Mr. Bodel\xe2\x80\x99s playin\xe2\x80\x99 kairds. But he neva\xe2\x80\x99\r\nstay atter one o\xe2\x80\x99clock. Come in, ma\xe2\x80\x99am; come in, suh; walk right \xe2\x80\x99long\r\nin.\xe2\x80\x9d He had drawn his own conclusions to explain their appearance. They\r\nstood upon the narrow porch waiting while he went inside to light the\r\nlamp.\r\n\r\nAlthough the house was small, as it comprised but one room, that room\r\nwas comparatively a large one. It looked to Tel\xc3\xa8sphore and Za\xc3\xafda very\r\nlarge and gloomy when they entered it. The lamp was on a table that\r\nstood against the wall, and that held further a rusty looking ink\r\nbottle, a pen and an old blank book. A narrow bed was off in the corner.\r\nThe brick chimney extended into the room and formed a ledge that served\r\nas mantel shelf. From the big, low-hanging rafters swung an assortment\r\nof fishing tackle, a gun, some discarded articles of clothing and a\r\nstring of red peppers. The boards of the floor were broad, rough and\r\nloosely joined together.\r\n\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore and Za\xc3\xafda seated themselves on opposite sides of the table\r\nand the negro went out to the wood pile to gather chips and pieces of\r\nbois-gras with which to kindle a small fire.\r\n\r\nIt was a little chilly; he supposed the two would want coffee and he\r\nknew that Wat Gibson would ask for a cup the first thing on his arrival.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder w\xe2\x80\x99at\xe2\x80\x99s keepin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99im,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Za\xc3\xafda impatiently. Tel\xc3\xa8sphore\r\nlooked at his watch. He had been looking at it at intervals of one\r\nminute straight along.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s ten minutes pas\xe2\x80\x99 one,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. He offered no further comment.\r\n\r\nAt twelve minutes past one Za\xc3\xafda\xe2\x80\x99s restlessness again broke into speech.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine, me, w\xe2\x80\x99at\xe2\x80\x99s become of Andr\xc3\xa9! He said he\xe2\x80\x99d be yere sho\xe2\x80\x99\r\nat one.\xe2\x80\x9d The old negro was kneeling before the fire that he had kindled,\r\ncontemplating the cheerful blaze. He rolled his eyes toward Za\xc3\xafda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou talkin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99bout Mr. Andr\xc3\xa9 Pascal? No need to look fo\xe2\x80\x99 him. Mr. Andre\r\nhe b\xe2\x80\x99en down to de P\xe2\x80\x99int all day raisin\xe2\x80\x99 Cain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a lie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Za\xc3\xafda. Tel\xc3\xa8sphore said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTain\xe2\x80\x99t no lie, ma\xe2\x80\x99am; he b\xe2\x80\x99en sho\xe2\x80\x99 raisin\xe2\x80\x99 de ole Nick.\xe2\x80\x9d She looked at\r\nhim, too contemptuous to reply.\r\n\r\nThe negro told no lie so far as his bald statement was concerned. He was\r\nsimply mistaken in his estimate of Andr\xc3\xa9 Pascal\xe2\x80\x99s ability to \xe2\x80\x9craise\r\nCain\xe2\x80\x9d during an entire afternoon and evening and still keep a rendezvous\r\nwith a lady at one o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning. For Andr\xc3\xa9 was even then at\r\nhand, as the loud and menacing howl of the dog testified. The negro\r\nhastened out to admit him.\r\n\r\nAndr\xc3\xa9 did not enter at once; he stayed a while outside abusing the dog\r\nand communicating to the negro his intention of coming out to shoot the\r\nanimal after he had attended to more pressing business that was awaiting\r\nhim within.\r\n\r\nZa\xc3\xafda arose, a little flurried and excited when he entered. Tel\xc3\xa8sphore\r\nremained seated.\r\n\r\nPascal was partially sober. There had evidently been an attempt at\r\ndressing for the occasion at some early part of the previous day, but\r\nsuch evidences had almost wholly vanished. His linen was soiled and his\r\nwhole appearance was that of a man who, by an effort, had aroused\r\nhimself from a debauch. He was a little taller than Tel\xc3\xa8sphore, and more\r\nloosely put together. Most women would have called him a handsomer man.\r\nIt was easy to imagine that when sober, he might betray by some subtle\r\ngrace of speech or manner, evidences of gentle blood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99y did you keep me waitin\xe2\x80\x99, Andr\xc3\xa9? w\xe2\x80\x99en you knew\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d she got no further,\r\nbut backed up against the table and stared at him with earnest, startled\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep you waiting, Za\xc3\xafda? my dear li\xe2\x80\x99le Za\xc3\xafd\xc3\xa9, how can you say such a\r\nthing! I started up yere an hour ago an\xe2\x80\x99 that\xe2\x80\x94w\xe2\x80\x99ere\xe2\x80\x99s that damned ole\r\nGibson?\xe2\x80\x9d He had approached Za\xc3\xafda with the evident intention of embracing\r\nher, but she seized his wrist and held him at arm\xe2\x80\x99s length away. In\r\ncasting his eyes about for old Gibson his glance alighted upon\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore.\r\n\r\nThe sight of the \xe2\x80\x99Cadian seemed to fill him with astonishment. He stood\r\nback and began to contemplate the young fellow and lose himself in\r\nspeculation and conjecture before him, as if before some unlabeled wax\r\nfigure. He turned for information to Za\xc3\xafda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Za\xc3\xafda, w\xe2\x80\x99at you call this? Wat kine of damn fool you got sitting\r\nyere? Who let him in? W\xe2\x80\x99at you reckon he\xe2\x80\x99s lookin\xe2\x80\x99 fo\xe2\x80\x99? trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore said nothing; he was awaiting his cue from Za\xc3\xafda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAndr\xc3\xa9 Pascal,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou jus\xe2\x80\x99 as well take the do\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 go. You\r\nmight stan\xe2\x80\x99 yere till the day o\xe2\x80\x99 judgment on yo\xe2\x80\x99 knees befo\xe2\x80\x99 me; an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nblow out yo\xe2\x80\x99 brains if you a mine to. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t neva goin\xe2\x80\x99 to marry you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe hell you ain\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had hardly more than uttered the words when he lay prone on his back.\r\nTel\xc3\xa8sphore had knocked him down. The blow seemed to complete the process\r\nof sobering that had begun in him. He gathered himself together and rose\r\nto his feet; in doing so he reached back for his pistol. His hold was\r\nnot yet steady, however, and the weapon slipped from his grasp and fell\r\nto the floor. Za\xc3\xafda picked it up and laid it on the table behind her.\r\nShe was going to see fair play.\r\n\r\nThe brute instinct that drives men at each other\xe2\x80\x99s throat was awake and\r\nstirring in these two. Each saw in the other a thing to be wiped out of\r\nhis way\xe2\x80\x94out of existence if need be. Passion and blind rage directed the\r\nblows which they dealt, and steeled the tension of muscles and clutch of\r\nfingers. They were not skillful blows, however.\r\n\r\nThe fire blazed cheerily; the kettle which the negro had placed upon the\r\ncoals was steaming and singing. The man had gone in search of his\r\nmaster. Za\xc3\xafda had placed the lamp out of harm\xe2\x80\x99s way on the high mantel\r\nledge and she leaned back with her hands behind her upon the table.\r\n\r\nShe did not raise her voice or lift her finger to stay the combat that\r\nwas acting before her. She was motionless, and white to the lips; only\r\nher eyes seemed to be alive and burning and blazing. At one moment she\r\nfelt that Andr\xc3\xa9 must have strangled Tel\xc3\xa8sphore; but she said nothing.\r\nThe next instant she could hardly doubt that the blow from Tel\xc3\xa8sphore\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndoubled fist could be less than a killing one; but she did nothing.\r\n\r\nHow the loose boards swayed and creaked beneath the weight of the\r\nstruggling men! the very old rafters seemed to groan; and she felt that\r\nthe house shook.\r\n\r\nThe combat, if fierce, was short, and it ended out on the gallery\r\nwhither they had staggered through the open door\xe2\x80\x94or one had dragged the\r\nother\xe2\x80\x94she could not tell. But she knew when it was over, for there was a\r\nlong moment of utter stillness. Then she heard one of the men descend\r\nthe steps and go away, for the gate slammed after him. The other went\r\nout to the cistern; the sound of the tin bucket splashing in the water\r\nreached her where she stood. He must have been endeavoring to remove\r\ntraces of the encounter.\r\n\r\nPresently Tel\xc3\xa8sphore entered the room. The elegance of his apparel had\r\nbeen somewhat marred; the men over at the \xe2\x80\x99Cadian ball would hardly have\r\ntaken exception now to his appearance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99ere is Andr\xc3\xa9?\xe2\x80\x9d the girl asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s gone,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tel\xc3\xa8sphore.\r\n\r\nShe had never changed her position and now when she drew herself up her\r\nwrists ached and she rubbed them a little. She was no longer pale; the\r\nblood had come back into her cheeks and lips, staining them crimson. She\r\nheld out her hand to him. He took it gratefully enough, but he did not\r\nknow what to do with it; that is, he did not know what he might dare to\r\ndo with it, so he let it drop gently away and went to the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon we betta be goin\xe2\x80\x99, too,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. He stooped and poured some\r\nof the bubbling water from the kettle upon the coffee which the negro\r\nhad set upon the hearth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll make a li\xe2\x80\x99le coffee firs\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d he proposed, \xe2\x80\x9can\xe2\x80\x99 anyhow we betta wait\r\ntill ole man w\xe2\x80\x99at\xe2\x80\x99shis-name comes back. It wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t look well to leave\r\nhis house that way without some kine of excuse or explanation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe made no reply, but seated herself submissively beside the table.\r\n\r\nHer will, which had been overmastering and aggressive, seemed to have\r\ngrown numb under the disturbing spell of the past few hours. An illusion\r\nhad gone from her, and had carried her love with it. The absence of\r\nregret revealed this to her. She realized, but could not comprehend it,\r\nnot knowing that the love had been part of the illusion. She was tired\r\nin body and spirit, and it was with a sense of restfulness that she sat\r\nall drooping and relaxed and watched Tel\xc3\xa8sphore make the coffee.\r\n\r\nHe made enough for them both and a cup for old Wat Gibson when he should\r\ncome in, and also one for the negro. He supposed the cups, the sugar and\r\nspoons were in the safe over there in the corner, and that is where he\r\nfound them.\r\n\r\nWhen he finally said to Za\xc3\xafda, \xe2\x80\x9cCome, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to take you home now,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand drew her shawl around her, pinning it under the chin, she was like a\r\nlittle child and followed whither he led in all confidence.\r\n\r\nIt was Tel\xc3\xa8sphore who drove on the way back, and he let the pony cut no\r\ncapers, but held him to a steady and tempered gait. The girl was still\r\nquiet and silent; she was thinking tenderly\xe2\x80\x94a little tearfully of those\r\ntwo old t\xc3\xaates-de-mulets yonder on Bayou de Glaize.\r\n\r\nHow they crept through the woods! and how dark it was and how still!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99at time it is?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Za\xc3\xafda. Alas! he could not tell her; his\r\nwatch was broken. But almost for the first time in his life, Tel\xc3\xa8sphore\r\ndid not care what time it was.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                               Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse\r\n\r\n                               Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse\r\n\r\n\r\n                                   I.\r\n\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse went away in the morning to make a visit to her parents, ten\r\nmiles back on rigolet de Bon Dieu. She did not return in the evening,\r\nand Cazeau, her husband, fretted not a little. He did not worry much\r\nabout Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse, who, he suspected, was resting only too content in the\r\nbosom of her family; his chief solicitude was manifestly for the pony\r\nshe had ridden. He felt sure those \xe2\x80\x9clazy pigs,\xe2\x80\x9d her brothers, were\r\ncapable of neglecting it seriously. This misgiving Cazeau communicated\r\nto his servant, old F\xc3\xa9licit\xc3\xa9, who waited upon him at supper.\r\n\r\nHis voice was low pitched, and even softer than F\xc3\xa9licit\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s. He was tall,\r\nsinewy, swarthy, and altogether severe looking. His thick black hair\r\nwaved, and it gleamed like the breast of a crow. The sweep of his\r\nmustache, which was not so black, outlined the broad contour of the\r\nmouth. Beneath the under lip grew a small tuft which he was much given\r\nto twisting, and which he permitted to grow, apparently for no other\r\npurpose. Cazeau\xe2\x80\x99s eyes were dark blue, narrow and overshadowed. His\r\nhands were coarse and stiff from close acquaintance with farming tools\r\nand implements, and he handled his fork and knife clumsily. But he was\r\ndistinguished looking, and succeeded in commanding a good deal of\r\nrespect, and even fear sometimes.\r\n\r\nHe ate his supper alone, by the light of a single coal-oil lamp that but\r\nfaintly illuminated the big room, with its bare floor and huge rafters,\r\nand its heavy pieces of furniture that loomed dimly in the gloom of the\r\napartment. F\xc3\xa9licit\xc3\xa9, ministering to his wants, hovered about the table\r\nlike a little, bent, restless shadow.\r\n\r\nShe served him with a dish of sunfish fried crisp and brown. There was\r\nnothing else set before him beside the bread and butter and the bottle\r\nof red wine which she locked carefully in the buffet after he had poured\r\nhis second glass. She was occupied with her mistress\xe2\x80\x99s absence, and kept\r\nreverting to it after he had expressed his solicitude about the pony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDat beat me! on\xe2\x80\x99y marry two mont\xe2\x80\x99, an\xe2\x80\x99 got de head turn\xe2\x80\x99 a\xe2\x80\x99ready to go\r\n\xe2\x80\x99broad. C\xe2\x80\x99est pas Chr\xc3\xa9tien, t\xc3\xa9nez!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCazeau shrugged his shoulders for answer, after he had drained his glass\r\nand pushed aside his plate. F\xc3\xa9licit\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s opinion of the unchristian-like\r\nbehavior of his wife in leaving him thus alone after two months of\r\nmarriage weighed little with him. He was used to solitude, and did not\r\nmind a day or a night or two of it. He had lived alone ten years, since\r\nhis first wife died, and F\xc3\xa9licit\xc3\xa9 might have known better than to\r\nsuppose that he cared. He told her she was a fool. It sounded like a\r\ncompliment in his modulated, caressing voice. She grumbled to herself as\r\nshe set about clearing the table, and Cazeau arose and walked outside on\r\nthe gallery; his spur, which he had not removed upon entering the house,\r\njangled at every step.\r\n\r\nThe night was beginning to deepen, and to gather black about the\r\nclusters of trees and shrubs that were grouped in the yard. In the beam\r\nof light from the open kitchen door a black boy stood feeding a brace of\r\nsnarling, hungry dogs; further away, on the steps of a cabin, some one\r\nwas playing the accordion; and in still another direction a little negro\r\nbaby was crying lustily. Cazeau walked around to the front of the house,\r\nwhich was square, squat and one-story.\r\n\r\nA belated wagon was driving in at the gate, and the impatient driver was\r\nswearing hoarsely at his jaded oxen. F\xc3\xa9licit\xc3\xa9 stepped out on the\r\ngallery, glass and polishing towel in hand, to investigate, and to\r\nwonder, too, who could be singing out on the river. It was a party of\r\nyoung people paddling around, waiting for the moon to rise, and they\r\nwere singing Juanita, their voices coming tempered and melodious through\r\nthe distance and the night.\r\n\r\nCazeau\xe2\x80\x99s horse was waiting, saddled, ready to be mounted, for Cazeau had\r\nmany things to attend to before bed-time; so many things that there was\r\nnot left to him a moment in which to think of Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse. He felt her\r\nabsence, though, like a dull, insistent pain.\r\n\r\nHowever, before he slept that night he was visited by the thought of\r\nher, and by a vision of her fair young face with its drooping lips and\r\nsullen and averted eyes. The marriage had been a blunder; he had only to\r\nlook into her eyes to feel that, to discover her growing aversion. But\r\nit was a thing not by any possibility to be undone. He was quite\r\nprepared to make the best of it, and expected no less than a like effort\r\non her part. The less she revisited the rigolet, the better. He would\r\nfind means to keep her at home hereafter.\r\n\r\nThese unpleasant reflections kept Cazeau awake far into the night,\r\nnotwithstanding the craving of his whole body for rest and sleep. The\r\nmoon was shining, and its pale effulgence reached dimly into the room,\r\nand with it a touch of the cool breath of the spring night. There was an\r\nunusual stillness abroad; no sound to be heard save the distant,\r\ntireless, plaintive notes of the accordion.\r\n\r\n                                  II.\r\n\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse did not return the following day, even though her husband sent\r\nher word to do so by her brother, Mont\xc3\xa9clin, who passed on his way to\r\nthe village early in the morning.\r\n\r\nOn the third day Cazeau saddled his horse and went himself in search of\r\nher. She had sent no word, no message, explaining her absence, and he\r\nfelt that he had good cause to be offended. It was rather awkward to\r\nhave to leave his work, even though late in the afternoon,\xe2\x80\x94Cazeau had\r\nalways so much to do; but among the many urgent calls upon him, the task\r\nof bringing his wife back to a sense of her duty seemed to him for the\r\nmoment paramount.\r\n\r\nThe Mich\xc3\xa9s, Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse\xe2\x80\x99s parents, lived on the old Gotrain place. It did\r\nnot belong to them; they were \xe2\x80\x9crunning\xe2\x80\x9d it for a merchant in Alexandria.\r\nThe house was far too big for their use. One of the lower rooms served\r\nfor the storing of wood and tools; the person \xe2\x80\x9coccupying\xe2\x80\x9d the place\r\nbefore Mich\xc3\xa9 having pulled up the flooring in despair of being able to\r\npatch it. Upstairs, the rooms were so large, so bare, that they offered\r\na constant temptation to lovers of the dance, whose importunities Madame\r\nMich\xc3\xa9 was accustomed to meet with amiable indulgence. A dance at Mich\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nand a plate of Madame Mich\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s gumbo fil\xc3\xa9 at midnight were pleasures not\r\nto be neglected or despised, unless by such serious souls as Cazeau.\r\n\r\nLong before Cazeau reached the house his approach had been observed, for\r\nthere was nothing to obstruct the view of the outer road; vegetation was\r\nnot yet abundantly advanced, and there was but a patchy, straggling\r\nstand of cotton and corn in Mich\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s field.\r\n\r\nMadame Mich\xc3\xa9, who had been seated on the gallery in a rocking-chair,\r\nstood up to greet him as he drew near. She was short and fat, and wore a\r\nblack skirt and loose muslin sack fastened at the throat with a hair\r\nbrooch. Her own hair, brown and glossy, showed but a few threads of\r\nsilver. Her round pink face was cheery, and her eyes were bright and\r\ngood humored. But she was plainly perturbed and ill at ease as Cazeau\r\nadvanced.\r\n\r\nMont\xc3\xa9clin, who was there too, was not ill at ease, and made no attempt\r\nto disguise the dislike with which his brother-in-law inspired him. He\r\nwas a slim, wiry fellow of twenty-five, short of stature like his\r\nmother, and resembling her in feature. He was in shirtsleeves, half\r\nleaning, half sitting, on the insecure railing of the gallery, and\r\nfanning himself with his broad-rimmed felt hat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCochon!\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered under his breath as Cazeau mounted the\r\nstairs,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9csacr\xc3\xa9 cochon!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCochon\xe2\x80\x9d had sufficiently characterized the man who had once on a time\r\ndeclined to lend Mont\xc3\xa9clin money. But when this same man had had the\r\npresumption to propose marriage to his well-beloved sister, Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse,\r\nand the honor to be accepted by her, Mont\xc3\xa9clin felt that a qualifying\r\nepithet was needed fully to express his estimate of Cazeau.\r\n\r\nMich\xc3\xa9 and his oldest son were absent. They both esteemed Cazeau highly,\r\nand talked much of his qualities of head and heart, and thought much of\r\nhis excellent standing with city merchants.\r\n\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse had shut herself up in her room. Cazeau had seen her rise and\r\nenter the house at perceiving him. He was a good deal mystified, but no\r\none could have guessed it when he shook hands with Madame Mich\xc3\xa9. He had\r\nonly nodded to Mont\xc3\xa9clin, with a muttered \xe2\x80\x9cComment \xc3\xa7a va?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTiens! something tole me you were coming to-day!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Madame\r\nMich\xc3\xa9, with a little blustering appearance of being cordial and at ease,\r\nas she offered Cazeau a chair.\r\n\r\nHe ventured a short laugh as he seated himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know, nothing would do,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, with much gesture of her\r\nsmall, plump hands, \xe2\x80\x9cnothing would do but Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse mus\xe2\x80\x99 stay las\xe2\x80\x99 night\r\nfo\xe2\x80\x99 a li\xe2\x80\x99le dance. The boys wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 year to their sister leaving.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCazeau shrugged his shoulders significantly, telling as plainly as words\r\nthat he knew nothing about it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cComment. Mont\xc3\xa9clin didn\xe2\x80\x99 tell you we were going to keep Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nMont\xc3\xa9clin had evidently told nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 how about the night befo\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d questioned Cazeau, \xe2\x80\x9can\xe2\x80\x99 las\xe2\x80\x99 night? It\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t possible you dance every night out yere on the Bon Dieu!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame Mich\xc3\xa9 laughed, with amiable appreciation of the sarcasm; and\r\nturning to her son, \xe2\x80\x9cMont\xc3\xa9clin, my boy, go tell yo\xe2\x80\x99 sister that Monsieur\r\nCazeau is yere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMont\xc3\xa9clin did not stir except to shift his position and settle himself\r\nmore securely on the railing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you year me, Mont\xc3\xa9clin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, I yeard you plain enough,\xe2\x80\x9d responded her son, \xe2\x80\x9cbut you know as\r\nwell as me it\xe2\x80\x99s no use to tell \xe2\x80\x99Th\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse anything. You been talkin\xe2\x80\x99 to\r\nher yo\xe2\x80\x99se\xe2\x80\x99f since Monday; an\xe2\x80\x99 pa\xe2\x80\x99s preached himse\xe2\x80\x99f hoa\xe2\x80\x99se on the\r\nsubject; an\xe2\x80\x99 you even had uncle Achille down yere yesterday to reason\r\nwith her. Wen \xe2\x80\x99Th\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse said she wasn\xe2\x80\x99 goin\xe2\x80\x99 to set her foot back in\r\nCazeau\xe2\x80\x99s house, she meant it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis speech, which Mont\xc3\xa9clin delivered with thorough unconcern, threw\r\nhis mother into a condition of painful but dumb embarrassment. It\r\nbrought two fiery red spots to Cazeau\xe2\x80\x99s cheeks, and for the space of a\r\nmoment he looked wicked.\r\n\r\nWhat Mont\xc3\xa9clin had spoken was quite true, though his taste in the manner\r\nand choice of time and place in saying it were not of the best.\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse, upon the first day of her arrival, had announced that she\r\ncame to stay, having no intention of returning under Cazeau\xe2\x80\x99s roof. The\r\nannouncement had scattered consternation, as she knew it would. She had\r\nbeen implored, scolded, entreated, stormed at, until she felt herself\r\nlike a dragging sail that all the winds of heaven had beaten upon. Why\r\nin the name of God had she married Cazeau? Her father had lashed her\r\nwith the question a dozen times. Why indeed? It was difficult now for\r\nher to understand why, unless because she supposed it was customary for\r\ngirls to marry when the right opportunity came. Cazeau, she knew, would\r\nmake life more comfortable for her; and again, she had liked him, and\r\nhad even been rather flustered when he pressed her hands and kissed\r\nthem, and kissed her lips and cheeks and eyes, when she accepted him.\r\n\r\nMont\xc3\xa9clin himself had taken her aside to talk the thing over. The turn\r\nof affairs was delighting him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, now, \xe2\x80\x99Th\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse, you mus\xe2\x80\x99 explain to me all about it, so we can\r\nsettle on a good cause, an\xe2\x80\x99 secu\xe2\x80\x99 a separation fo\xe2\x80\x99 you. Has he been\r\nmistreating an\xe2\x80\x99 abusing you, the sacr\xc3\xa9 cochon?\xe2\x80\x9d They were alone together\r\nin her room, whither she had taken refuge from the angry domestic\r\nelements.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou please to reserve yo\xe2\x80\x99 disgusting expressions, Mont\xc3\xa9clin. No, he has\r\nnot abused me in any way that I can think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he drink? Come \xe2\x80\x99Th\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse, think well over it. Does he ever get\r\ndrunk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrunk! Oh, mercy, no,\xe2\x80\x94Cazeau never gets drunk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see; it\xe2\x80\x99s jus\xe2\x80\x99 simply you feel like me; you hate him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t hate him,\xe2\x80\x9d she returned reflectively; adding with a sudden\r\nimpulse, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s jus\xe2\x80\x99 being married that I detes\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 despise. I hate\r\nbeing Mrs. Cazeau, an\xe2\x80\x99 would want to be Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse Mich\xc3\xa9 again. I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstan\xe2\x80\x99 to live with a man; to have him always there; his coats an\xe2\x80\x99\r\npantaloons hanging in my room; his ugly bare feet\xe2\x80\x94washing them in my\r\ntub, befo\xe2\x80\x99 my very eyes, ugh!\xe2\x80\x9d She shuddered with recollections, and\r\nresumed, with a sigh that was almost a sob: \xe2\x80\x9cMon Dieu, mon Dieu! Sister\r\nMarie Ang\xc3\xa9lique knew w\xe2\x80\x99at she was saying; she knew me better than myse\xe2\x80\x99f\r\nw\xe2\x80\x99en she said God had sent me a vocation an\xe2\x80\x99 I was turning deaf ears.\r\nW\xe2\x80\x99en I think of a blessed life in the convent, at peace! Oh, w\xe2\x80\x99at was I\r\ndreaming of!\xe2\x80\x9d and then the tears came.\r\n\r\nMont\xc3\xa9clin felt disconcerted and greatly disappointed at having obtained\r\nevidence that would carry no weight with a court of justice. The day had\r\nnot come when a young woman might ask the court\xe2\x80\x99s permission to return\r\nto her mamma on the sweeping ground of a constitutional disinclination\r\nfor marriage. But if there was no way of untying this Gordian knot of\r\nmarriage, there was surely a way of cutting it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, \xe2\x80\x99Th\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse, I\xe2\x80\x99m mighty durn sorry yo got no better groun\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x99an\r\nw\xe2\x80\x99at you say. But you can count on me to stan\xe2\x80\x99 by you w\xe2\x80\x99atever you do.\r\nGod knows I don\xe2\x80\x99 blame you fo\xe2\x80\x99 not wantin\xe2\x80\x99 to live with Cazeau.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd now there was Cazeau himself, with the red spots flaming in his\r\nswarthy cheeks, looking and feeling as if he wanted to thrash Mont\xc3\xa9clin\r\ninto some semblance of decency. He arose abruptly, and approaching the\r\nroom which he had seen his wife enter, thrust open the door after a\r\nhasty preliminary knock. Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse, who was standing erect at a far\r\nwindow, turned at his entrance.\r\n\r\nShe appeared neither angry nor frightened, but thoroughly unhappy, with\r\nan appeal in her soft dark eyes and a tremor on her lips that seemed to\r\nhim expressions of unjust reproach, that wounded and maddened him at\r\nonce. But whatever he might feel, Cazeau knew only one way to act toward\r\na woman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse, you are not ready?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked in his quiet tones. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngetting late; we havn\xe2\x80\x99 any time to lose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe knew that Mont\xc3\xa9clin had spoken out, and she had hoped for a wordy\r\ninterview, a stormy scene, in which she might have held her own as she\r\nhad held it for the past three days against her family, with Mont\xc3\xa9clin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naid. But she had no weapon with which to combat subtlety. Her husband\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlooks, his tones, his mere presence, brought to her a sudden sense of\r\nhopelessness, an instinctive realization of the futility of rebellion\r\nagainst a social and sacred institution.\r\n\r\nCazeau said nothing further, but stood waiting in the doorway. Madame\r\nMich\xc3\xa9 had walked to the far end of the gallery, and pretended to be\r\noccupied with having a chicken driven from her parterre. Mont\xc3\xa9clin stood\r\nby, exasperated, fuming, ready to burst out.\r\n\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse went and reached for her riding skirt that hung against the\r\nwall. She was rather tall, with a figure which, though not robust,\r\nseemed perfect in its fine proportions. \xe2\x80\x9cLa fille de son p\xc3\xa8re,\xe2\x80\x9d she was\r\noften called, which was a great compliment to Mich\xc3\xa9. Her brown hair was\r\nbrushed all fluffily back from her temples and low forehead, and about\r\nher features and expression lurked a softness, a prettiness, a dewiness,\r\nthat were perhaps too childlike, that savored of immaturity.\r\n\r\nShe slipped the riding-skirt, which was of black alpaca, over her head,\r\nand with impatient fingers hooked it at the waist over her pink\r\nlinen-lawn. Then she fastened on her white sunbonnet and reached for her\r\ngloves on the mantelpiece.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99 wan\xe2\x80\x99 to go, you know w\xe2\x80\x99at you got to do, \xe2\x80\x99Th\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse,\xe2\x80\x9d fumed\r\nMont\xc3\xa9clin. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99 set yo\xe2\x80\x99 feet back on Cane River, by God, unless you\r\nwant to,\xe2\x80\x94not w\xe2\x80\x99ile I\xe2\x80\x99m alive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCazeau looked at him as if he were a monkey whose antics fell short of\r\nbeing amusing.\r\n\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse still made no reply, said not a word. She walked rapidly past\r\nher husband, past her brother; bidding good-bye to no one, not even to\r\nher mother. She descended the stairs, and without assistance from any\r\none mounted the pony, which Cazeau had ordered to be saddled upon his\r\narrival. In this way she obtained a fair start of her husband, whose\r\ndeparture was far more leisurely, and for the greater part of the way\r\nshe managed to keep an appreciable gap between them. She rode almost\r\nmadly at first, with the wind inflating her skirt balloon-like about her\r\nknees, and her sunbonnet falling back between her shoulders.\r\n\r\nAt no time did Cazeau make an effort to overtake her until traversing an\r\nold fallow meadow that was level and hard as a table. The sight of a\r\ngreat solitary oak-tree, with its seemingly immutable outlines, that had\r\nbeen a landmark for ages\xe2\x80\x94or was it the odor of elderberry stealing up\r\nfrom the gully to the south? or what was it that brought vividly back to\r\nCazeau, by some association of ideas, a scene of many years ago? He had\r\npassed that old live-oak hundreds of times, but it was only now that the\r\nmemory of one day came back to him. He was a very small boy that day,\r\nseated before his father on horseback. They were proceeding slowly, and\r\nBlack Gabe was moving on before them at a little dog-trot. Black Gabe\r\nhad run away, and had been discovered back in the Gotrain swamp. They\r\nhad halted beneath this big oak to enable the negro to take breath; for\r\nCazeau\xe2\x80\x99s father was a kind and considerate master, and every one had\r\nagreed at the time that Black Gabe was a fool, a great idiot indeed, for\r\nwanting to run away from him.\r\n\r\nThe whole impression was for some reason hideous, and to dispel it\r\nCazeau spurred his horse to a swift gallop. Overtaking his wife, he rode\r\nthe remainder of the way at her side in silence.\r\n\r\nIt was late when they reached home. F\xc3\xa9licit\xc3\xa9 was standing on the grassy\r\nedge of the road, in the moonlight, waiting for them.\r\n\r\nCazeau once more ate his supper alone; for Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse went to her room,\r\nand there she was crying again.\r\n\r\n                                  III.\r\n\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse was not one to accept the inevitable with patient resignation,\r\na talent born in the souls of many women; neither was she the one to\r\naccept it with philosophical resignation, like her husband. Her\r\nsensibilities were alive and keen and responsive. She met the\r\npleasurable things of life with frank, open appreciation, and against\r\ndistasteful conditions she rebelled. Dissimulation was as foreign to her\r\nnature as guile to the breast of a babe, and her rebellious outbreaks,\r\nby no means rare, had hitherto been quite open and aboveboard. People\r\noften said that Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse would know her own mind some day, which was\r\nequivalent to saying that she was at present unacquainted with it. If\r\nshe ever came to such knowledge, it would be by no intellectual\r\nresearch, by no subtle analyses or tracing the motives of actions to\r\ntheir source. It would come to her as the song to the bird, the perfume\r\nand color to the flower.\r\n\r\nHer parents had hoped\xe2\x80\x94not without reason and justice\xe2\x80\x94that marriage would\r\nbring the poise, the desirable pose, so glaringly lacking in Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncharacter. Marriage they knew to be a wonderful and powerful agent in\r\nthe development and formation of a woman\xe2\x80\x99s character; they had seen its\r\neffect too often to doubt it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if this marriage does nothing else,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mich\xc3\xa9 in an outburst\r\nof sudden exasperation, \xe2\x80\x9cit will rid us of Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse; for I am at the\r\nend of my patience with her! You have never had the firmness to manage\r\nher,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he was speaking to his wife,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI have not had the time, the\r\nleisure, to devote to her training; and what good we might have\r\naccomplished, that maudit Mont\xc3\xa9clin\xe2\x80\x94Well, Cazeau is the one! It takes\r\njust such a steady hand to guide a disposition like Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse\xe2\x80\x99s, a\r\nmaster hand, a strong will that compels obedience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd now, when they had hoped for so much, here was Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse, with\r\ngathered and fierce vehemence, beside which her former outbursts\r\nappeared mild, declaring that she would not, and she would not, and she\r\nwould not continue to enact the r\xc3\xb4le of wife to Cazeau. If she had had a\r\nreason! as Madame Mich\xc3\xa9 lamented; but it could not be discovered that\r\nshe had any sane one. He had never scolded, or called names, or deprived\r\nher of comforts, or been guilty of any of the many reprehensible acts\r\ncommonly attributed to objectionable husbands. He did not slight nor\r\nneglect her. Indeed, Cazeau\xe2\x80\x99s chief offense seemed to be that he loved\r\nher, and Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse was not the woman to be loved against her will. She\r\ncalled marriage a trap set for the feet of unwary and unsuspecting\r\ngirls, and in round, unmeasured terms reproached her mother with\r\ntreachery and deceit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you Cazeau was the man,\xe2\x80\x9d chuckled Mich\xc3\xa9, when his wife had\r\nrelated the scene that had accompanied and influenced Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeparture.\r\n\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse again hoped, in the morning, that Cazeau would scold or make\r\nsome sort of a scene, but he apparently did not dream of it. It was\r\nexasperating that he should take her acquiescence so for granted. It is\r\ntrue he had been up and over the fields and across the river and back\r\nlong before she was out of bed, and he may have been thinking of\r\nsomething else, which was no excuse, which was even in some sense an\r\naggravation. But he did say to her at breakfast, \xe2\x80\x9cThat brother of yo\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nthat Mont\xc3\xa9clin, is unbearable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMont\xc3\xa9clin? Par exemple!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse, seated opposite to her husband, was attired in a white\r\nmorning wrapper. She wore a somewhat abused, long face, it is true,\xe2\x80\x94an\r\nexpression of countenance familiar to some husbands,\xe2\x80\x94but the expression\r\nwas not sufficiently pronounced to mar the charm of her youthful\r\nfreshness. She had little heart to eat, only playing with the food\r\nbefore her, and she felt a pang of resentment at her husband\xe2\x80\x99s healthy\r\nappetite.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Mont\xc3\xa9clin,\xe2\x80\x9d he reasserted. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s developed into a firs\xe2\x80\x99-class\r\nnuisance; an\xe2\x80\x99 you better tell him, Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse,\xe2\x80\x94unless you want me to tell\r\nhim,\xe2\x80\x94to confine his energies after this to matters that concern him. I\r\nhave no use fo\xe2\x80\x99 him or fo\xe2\x80\x99 his interference in w\xe2\x80\x99at regards you an\xe2\x80\x99 me\r\nalone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was said with unusual asperity. It was the little breach that\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse had been watching for, and she charged rapidly: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s strange,\r\nif you detes\xe2\x80\x99 Mont\xc3\xa9clin so heartily, that you would desire to marry his\r\nsister.\xe2\x80\x9d She knew it was a silly thing to say, and was not surprised\r\nwhen he told her so. It gave her a little foothold for further attack,\r\nhowever. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see, anyhow, w\xe2\x80\x99at reason you had to marry me, w\xe2\x80\x99en\r\nthere were so many others,\xe2\x80\x9d she complained, as if accusing him of\r\npersecution and injury. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was Marianne running after you fo\xe2\x80\x99 the\r\nlas\xe2\x80\x99 five years till it was disgraceful; an\xe2\x80\x99 any one of the Dortrand\r\ngirls would have been glad to marry you. But no, nothing would do; you\r\nmus\xe2\x80\x99 come out on the rigolet fo\xe2\x80\x99 me.\xe2\x80\x9d Her complaint was pathetic, and at\r\nthe same time so amusing that Cazeau was forced to smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t see w\xe2\x80\x99at the Dortrand girls or Marianne have to do with it,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nrejoined; adding, with no trace of amusement, \xe2\x80\x9cI married you because I\r\nloved you; because you were the woman I wanted to marry, an\xe2\x80\x99 the only\r\none. I reckon I tole you that befo\xe2\x80\x99. I thought\xe2\x80\x94of co\xe2\x80\x99se I was a fool fo\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntaking things fo\xe2\x80\x99 granted\xe2\x80\x94but I did think that I might make you happy in\r\nmaking things easier an\xe2\x80\x99 mo\xe2\x80\x99 comfortable fo\xe2\x80\x99 you. I expected\xe2\x80\x94I was even\r\nthat big a fool\xe2\x80\x94I believed that yo\xe2\x80\x99 coming yere to me would be like the\r\nsun shining out of the clouds, an\xe2\x80\x99 that our days would be like w\xe2\x80\x99at the\r\nstory-books promise after the wedding. I was mistaken. But I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nimagine w\xe2\x80\x99at induced you to marry me. W\xe2\x80\x99atever it was, I reckon you\r\nfoun\xe2\x80\x99 out you made a mistake, too. I don\xe2\x80\x99 see anything to do but make\r\nthe best of a bad bargain, an\xe2\x80\x99 shake han\xe2\x80\x99s over it.\xe2\x80\x9d He had arisen from\r\nthe table, and, approaching, held out his hand to her. What he had said\r\nwas commonplace enough, but it was significant, coming from Cazeau, who\r\nwas not often so unreserved in expressing himself.\r\n\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse ignored the hand held out to her. She was resting her chin in\r\nher palm, and kept her eyes fixed moodily upon the table. He rested his\r\nhand, that she would not touch, upon her head for an instant, and walked\r\naway out of the room.\r\n\r\nShe heard him giving orders to workmen who had been waiting for him out\r\non the gallery, and she heard him mount his horse and ride away. A\r\nhundred things would distract him and engage his attention during the\r\nday. She felt that he had perhaps put her and her grievance from his\r\nthoughts when he crossed the threshold; whilst she\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nOld F\xc3\xa9licit\xc3\xa9 was standing there holding a shining tin pail, asking for\r\nflour and lard and eggs from the storeroom, and meal for the chicks.\r\n\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse seized the bunch of keys which hung from her belt and flung\r\nthem at F\xc3\xa9licit\xc3\xa9\xe2\x80\x99s feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTiens! tu vas les garder comme tu as jadis fait. Je ne veux plus de ce\r\ntrain l\xc3\xa0, moi!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old woman stooped and picked up the keys from the floor. It was\r\nreally all one to her that her mistress returned them to her keeping,\r\nand refused to take further account of the m\xc3\xa9nage.\r\n\r\n                                  IV.\r\n\r\nIt seemed now to Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse that Mont\xc3\xa9clin was the only friend left to\r\nher in the world. Her father and mother had turned from her in what\r\nappeared to be her hour of need. Her friends laughed at her, and refused\r\nto take seriously the hints which she threw out,\xe2\x80\x94feeling her way to\r\ndiscover if marriage were as distasteful to other women as to herself.\r\nMont\xc3\xa9clin alone understood her. He alone had always been ready to act\r\nfor her and with her, to comfort and solace her with his sympathy and\r\nhis support. Her only hope for rescue from her hateful surroundings lay\r\nin Mont\xc3\xa9clin. Of herself she felt powerless to plan, to act, even to\r\nconceive a way out of this pitfall into which the whole world seemed to\r\nhave conspired to thrust her.\r\n\r\nShe had a great desire to see her brother, and wrote asking him to come\r\nto her. But it better suited Mont\xc3\xa9clin\xe2\x80\x99s spirit of adventure to appoint\r\na meeting-place at the turn of the lane, where Ath\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse might appear to\r\nbe walking leisurely for health and recreation, and where he might seem\r\nto be riding along, bent on some errand of business or pleasure.\r\n\r\nThere had been a shower, a sudden downpour, short as it was sudden, that\r\nhad laid the dust in the road. It had freshened the pointed leaves of\r\nthe live-oaks, and brightened up the big fields of cotton on either side\r\nof the lane till they seemed carpeted with green, glittering gems.\r\n\r\nAth\xc3\xa9na\xc3\xafse walked along the grassy edge of the road, lifting her crisp\r\nskirts with one hand, and with the other twirling a gay sunshade over\r\nher bare head. The scent of the fields after the rain was delicious. She\r\ninhaled long breaths of their freshness and perfume, that soothed and\r\nquieted her for the moment. There were birds splashing and spluttering\r\nin the pools, pluming themselves on the fence-*rails, and sending out\r\nlittle sharp cries, twitters, and shrill rhapsodies of delight.\r\n\r\nShe saw Mont\xc3\xa9clin approaching from a great distance,\xe2\x80\x94almost as far away\r\nas the turn of the woods. But she could not feel sure it was he; it\r\nappeared too tall for Mont\xc3\xa9clin, but that was because he was riding a\r\nlarge horse. She waved her parasol to him; she was so glad to see him.\r\nShe had never been so glad to see Mont\xc3\xa9clin before; not even the day\r\nwhen he had taken her out of the convent, against her parents\xe2\x80\x99 wishes,\r\nbecause she had expressed a desire to remain there no longer. He seemed\r\nto her, as he drew near, the embodiment of kindness, of bravery, of\r\nchivalry, even of wisdom; for she had never known Mont\xc3\xa9clin at a loss to\r\nextricate himself from a disagreeable situation.\r\n\r\nHe dismounted, and, leading his horse by the bridle, started to walk\r\nbeside her, after he had kissed her affectionately and asked her what\r\nshe was crying about. She protested that she was not crying, for she was\r\nlaughing, though drying her eyes at the same time on her handkerchief,\r\nrolled in a soft mop for the purpose.\r\n\r\nShe took Mont\xc3\xa9clin\xe2\x80\x99s arm, and they strolled slowly down the lane; they\r\ncould not seat themselves for a comfortable chat, as they would have\r\nliked, with the grass all sparkling and bristling wet.\r\n\r\nYes, she was quite as wretched as ever, she told him. The week which had\r\ngone by since she saw him had in no wise lightened the burden of her\r\ndiscontent.
