It is why I esteem that a blessed privilege to give her declining\r\nyears those little comforts, ces petits soins, that is a woman\xe2\x80\x99s right\r\nto expec\xe2\x80\x99 from men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI knew what \xe2\x80\x9cdes petits soins\xe2\x80\x9d meant with Cavanelle; doctors\xe2\x80\x99 visits,\r\nlittle jaunts across the lake, friandises of every description showered\r\nupon \xe2\x80\x9cAunt F\xc3\xa9licie,\xe2\x80\x9d and he himself relegated to the soup and bouillie\r\nwhich typified his prosaic existence.\r\n\r\nI was unreasonably exasperated with the man for awhile, and would not\r\neven permit myself to notice the beauty in texture and design of the\r\nmousseline de laine which he had spread across the counter in tempting\r\nfolds. I was forced to restrain a brutal desire to say something\r\nstinging and cruel to him for his fatuity.\r\n\r\nHowever, before I had regained the street, the conviction that Cavanelle\r\nwas a hopeless fool seemed to reconcile me to the situation and also\r\nafforded me some diversion.\r\n\r\nBut even this estimate of my poor Cavanelle was destined not to last. By\r\nthe time I had seated myself in the Prytania street car and passed up my\r\nnickel, I was convinced that Cavanelle was an angel.\r\n\r\n------------------------------------------------------------------------\r\n\r\n                           Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette\r\n\r\n------------------------------------------------------------------------\r\n\r\n                           Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette\r\n\r\n[Illustration]\r\n\r\nIt happened just as every one had predicted. Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette was\r\nbeside herself with rage and indignation when she learned that the town\r\nauthorities had for some reason condemned her house and intended to\r\ndemolish it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDat house w\xe2\x80\x99at Vieumaite gi\xe2\x80\x99 me his own se\xe2\x80\x99f, out his own mout\xe2\x80\x99, w\xe2\x80\x99en\r\nhe gi\xe2\x80\x99 me my freedom! All wrote down en r\xc3\xa8gle befo\xe2\x80\x99 de cote! Bon dieu\r\nSeigneur, w\xe2\x80\x99at dey talkin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99bout!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette stood in the doorway of her home, resting a gaunt\r\nblack hand against the jamb. In the other hand she held her corn-cob\r\npipe. She was a tall, large-boned woman of a pronounced Congo type. The\r\nhouse in question had been substantial enough in its time. It contained\r\nfour rooms: the lower two of brick, the upper ones of adobe. A\r\ndilapidated gallery projected from the upper story and slanted over the\r\nnarrow banquette, to the peril of passers-by.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I ever heard why the property was given to you in the\r\nfirst place, Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Lawyer Paxton, who had stopped\r\nin passing, as so many others did, to talk the matter over with the old\r\nnegress. The affair was attracting some attention in town, and its\r\ndevelopment was being watched with a good deal of interest. Tante\r\nCat\xe2\x80\x99rinette asked nothing better than to satisfy the lawyer\xe2\x80\x99s curiosity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVieumaite all time say. Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette wort\xe2\x80\x99 gole to \xe2\x80\x99im; de way I make\r\ndem nigga\xe2\x80\x99 walk chalk. But,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued, with recovered seriousness,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cw\xe2\x80\x99en I nuss \xe2\x80\x99is li\xe2\x80\x99le gal w\xe2\x80\x99at all de doctor\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99low it \xe2\x80\x99s goin\xe2\x80\x99 die, an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nI make it well, me, den Vieumaite, he can\xe2\x80\x99t do \xe2\x80\x99nough, him. He name\xe2\x80\x99 dat\r\nli\xe2\x80\x99le gal Cat\xe2\x80\x99rine fo\xe2\x80\x99 me. Das Miss Kitty w\xe2\x80\x99at marry Mich\xc3\xa9 Raymond yon\xe2\x80\x99\r\nby Gran\xe2\x80\x99 Eco\xe2\x80\x99. Den he gi\xe2\x80\x99 me my freedom; he got plenty slave\xe2\x80\x99, him; one\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99 count in his pocket. An\xe2\x80\x99 he gi\xe2\x80\x99 me dat house w\xe2\x80\x99at I\xe2\x80\x99m stan\xe2\x80\x99in\xe2\x80\x99 in\r\nde do\xe2\x80\x99; he got plenty house\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 lan\xe2\x80\x99, him. I waitin\xe2\x80\x99 fo\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em, Mich\xc3\xa9 Paxtone,\xe2\x80\x9d and a wicked gleam shot into the woman\xe2\x80\x99s small,\r\ndusky eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cI got my axe grine fine. Fus\xe2\x80\x99 man w\xe2\x80\x99at touch Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette\r\nfo\xe2\x80\x99 tu\xe2\x80\x99n her out dat house, he git \xe2\x80\x99is head bus\xe2\x80\x99 like I bus\xe2\x80\x99 a gode.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDat\xe2\x80\x99s nice day, ainty, Mich\xc3\xa9 Paxtone? Fine wedda fo\xe2\x80\x99 dry my close.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nUpon the gallery above hung an array of shirts, which gleamed white in\r\nthe sunshine, and flapped in the rippling breeze.\r\n\r\nThe spectacle of Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette defying the authorities was one which\r\noffered much diversion to the children of the neighborhood. They played\r\nnumberless pranks at her expense; daily serving upon her fictitious\r\nnotices purporting to be to the last degree official. One youngster, in\r\na moment of inspiration, composed a couplet, which they recited, sang,\r\nshouted at all hours, beneath her windows.\r\n\r\n               \xe2\x80\x9cTante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette, she go in town;\r\n               Wen she come back, her house pull\xe2\x80\x99 down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo ran the production. She heard it many times during the day, but, far\r\nfrom offending her, she accepted it as a warning,\xe2\x80\x94a prediction, as it\r\nwere,\xe2\x80\x94and she took heed not to offer to fate the conditions for its\r\nfulfillment. She no longer quitted her house even for a moment, so great\r\nwas her fear and so firm her belief that the town authorities were lying\r\nin wait to possess themselves of it. She would not cross the street to\r\nvisit a neighbor. She waylaid passers-by and pressed them into service\r\nto do her errands and small shopping. She grew distrustful and\r\nsuspicious, ever on the alert to scent a plot in the most innocent\r\nendeavor to induce her to leave the house.\r\n\r\nOne morning, as Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette was hanging out her latest batch of\r\nwashing, Eus\xc3\xa8be, a \xe2\x80\x9cfree mulatto\xe2\x80\x9d from Red River, stopped his pony\r\nbeneath her gallery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xc3\xa9, Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette!\xe2\x80\x9d he called up to her.\r\n\r\nShe turned to the railing just as she was, in her bare arms and neck\r\nthat gleamed ebony-like against the unbleached cotton of her chemise. A\r\ncoarse skirt was fastened about her waist, and a string of many-colored\r\nbeads knotted around her throat. She held her smoking pipe between her\r\nyellow teeth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow you all come on, Mich\xc3\xa9 Eus\xc3\xa8be?\xe2\x80\x9d she questioned, pleasantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe all middlin\xe2\x80\x99, Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette. But Miss Kitty, she putty bad off\r\nout yon\xe2\x80\x99a. I see Mista Raymond dis mo\xe2\x80\x99nin\xe2\x80\x99 w\xe2\x80\x99en I pass by his house; he\r\nsay look like de feva don\xe2\x80\x99 wan\xe2\x80\x99 to quit \xe2\x80\x99er. She been axen\xe2\x80\x99 fo\xe2\x80\x99 you all\r\nt\xe2\x80\x99rough de night. He \xe2\x80\x99low he reckon I betta tell you. Nice wedda we got\r\nfo\xe2\x80\x99 plantin\xe2\x80\x99, Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNice wedda fo\xe2\x80\x99 lies, Mich\xc3\xa9 Eus\xc3\xa8be,\xe2\x80\x9d and she spat contemptuously down\r\nupon the banquette. She turned away without noticing the man further,\r\nand proceeded to hang one of Lawyer Paxton\xe2\x80\x99s fine linen shirts upon the\r\nline.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe been axen\xe2\x80\x99 fo\xe2\x80\x99 you all t\xe2\x80\x99rough de night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomehow Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette could not get that refrain out of her head.\r\nShe would not willingly believe that Eus\xc3\xa8be had spoken the truth, but\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe been axen fo\xe2\x80\x99 you all t\xe2\x80\x99rough de night\xe2\x80\x94all t\xe2\x80\x99rough de night.\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nwords kept ringing in her ears, as she came and went about her daily\r\ntasks. But by degrees she dismissed Eus\xc3\xa8be and his message from her\r\nmind. It was Miss Kitty\xe2\x80\x99s voice that she could hear in fancy following\r\nher, calling out through the night, \xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99ere Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette? W\xe2\x80\x99y Tante\r\nCat\xe2\x80\x99rinette don\xe2\x80\x99 come? W\xe2\x80\x99y she don\xe2\x80\x99 come\xe2\x80\x94w\xe2\x80\x99y she don\xe2\x80\x99 come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll day the woman muttered and mumbled to herself in her Creole patois;\r\ninvoking council of \xe2\x80\x9cVieumaite,\xe2\x80\x9d as she always did in her troubles.\r\nTante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette\xe2\x80\x99s religion was peculiarly her own; she turned to\r\nheaven with her grievances, it is true, but she felt that there was no\r\none in Paradise with whom she was quite so well acquainted as with\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVieumaite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLate in the afternoon she went and stood on her doorstep, and looked\r\nuneasily and anxiously out upon the almost deserted street. When a\r\nlittle girl came walking by,\xe2\x80\x94a sweet child with a frank and innocent\r\nface, upon whose word she knew she could rely,\xe2\x80\x94Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette invited\r\nher to enter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome yere see Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette, Lolo. It\xe2\x80\x99s long time you en\xe2\x80\x99t come see\r\nTante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rine; you gittin\xe2\x80\x99 proud.\xe2\x80\x9d She made the little one sit down,\r\nand offered her a couple of cookies, which the child accepted with\r\npretty avidity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou putty good li\xe2\x80\x99le gal, you, Lolo. You keep on go confession all de\r\ntime?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. I\xe2\x80\x99m goin\xe2\x80\x99 make my firs\xe2\x80\x99 communion firs\xe2\x80\x99 of May, Tante\r\nCat\xe2\x80\x99rinette.\xe2\x80\x9d A dog-eared catechism was sticking out of Lolo\xe2\x80\x99s apron\r\npocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDas right; be good li\xe2\x80\x99le gal. Mine yo\xe2\x80\x99 maman ev\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x99ing she say; an\xe2\x80\x99 neva\r\ntell no story. It\xe2\x80\x99s nuttin\xe2\x80\x99 bad in dis worl\xe2\x80\x99 like tellin\xe2\x80\x99 lies. You know\r\nEus\xc3\xa8be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEus\xc3\xa8be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYas; dat li\xe2\x80\x99le ole Red River free m\xe2\x80\x99latto. Uh, uh! dat one man w\xe2\x80\x99at kin\r\ntell lies, yas! He come tell me Miss Kitty down sick yon\xe2\x80\x99a. You ev\xe2\x80\x99\r\nyeard such big story like dat, Lolo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe child looked a little bewildered, but she answered promptly, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Taint\r\nno story, Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette. I yeard papa sayin\xe2\x80\x99, dinner time, Mr.\r\nRaymond sen\xe2\x80\x99 fo\xe2\x80\x99 Dr. Chalon. An\xe2\x80\x99 Dr. Chalon says he ain\xe2\x80\x99t got time to go\r\nyonda. An\xe2\x80\x99 papa says it\xe2\x80\x99s because Dr. Chalon on\xe2\x80\x99y want to go w\xe2\x80\x99ere it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrich people; an\xe2\x80\x99 he\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x99fraid Mista Raymond ain\xe2\x80\x99 goin\xe2\x80\x99 pay \xe2\x80\x99im.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette admired the little girl\xe2\x80\x99s pretty gingham dress, and\r\nasked her who had ironed it. She stroked her brown curls, and talked of\r\nall manner of things quite foreign to the subject of Eus\xc3\xa8be and his\r\nwicked propensity for telling lies.\r\n\r\nShe was not restless as she had been during the early part of the day,\r\nand she no longer mumbled and muttered as she had been doing over her\r\nwork.\r\n\r\nAt night she lighted her coal-oil lamp, and placed it near a window\r\nwhere its light could be seen from the street through the half-closed\r\nshutters. Then she sat herself down, erect and motionless, in a chair.\r\n\r\nWhen it was near upon midnight, Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette arose, and looked\r\ncautiously, very cautiously, out of the door. Her house lay in the line\r\nof deep shadow that extended along the street. The other side was bathed\r\nin the pale light of the declining moon. The night was agreeably mild,\r\nprofoundly still, but pregnant with the subtle quivering life of early\r\nspring. The earth seemed asleep and breathing,\xe2\x80\x94a scent-laden breath that\r\nblew in soft puffs against Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette\xe2\x80\x99s face as she emerged from\r\nthe house. She closed and locked her door noiselessly; then she crept\r\nslowly away, treading softly, stealthily as a cat, in the deep shadow.\r\n\r\nThere were but few people abroad at that hour. Once she ran upon a gay\r\nparty of ladies and gentlemen who had been spending the evening over\r\ncards and anisette. They did not notice Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette almost\r\neffacing herself against the black wall of the cathedral. She breathed\r\nfreely and ventured from her retreat only when they had disappeared from\r\nview. Once a man saw her quite plainly, as she darted across a narrow\r\nstrip of moonlight. But Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette need not have gasped with\r\nfright as she did. He was too drunk to know if she were a thing of\r\nflesh, or only one of the fantastic, maddening shadows that the moon was\r\ncasting across his path to bewilder him. When she reached the outskirts\r\nof the town, and had to cross the broad piece of open country which\r\nstretched out toward the pine wood, an almost paralyzing terror came\r\nover her. But she crouched low, and hurried through the marsh and weeds,\r\navoiding the open road. She could have been mistaken for one of the\r\nbeasts browsing there where she passed.\r\n\r\nBut once in the Grand Ecore road that lay through the pine wood, she\r\nfelt secure and free to move as she pleased. Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette\r\nstraightened herself, stiffened herself in fact, and unconsciously\r\nassuming the attitude of the professional sprinter, she sped rapidly\r\nbeneath the Gothic interlacing branches of the pines. She talked\r\nconstantly to herself as she went, and to the animate and inanimate\r\nobjects around her. But her speech, far from intelligent, was hardly\r\nintelligible.\r\n\r\nShe addressed herself to the moon, which she apostrophized as an\r\nimpertinent busybody spying upon her actions. She pictured all manner of\r\ntroublesome animals, snakes, rabbits, frogs, pursuing her, but she\r\ndefied them to catch Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette, who was hurrying toward Miss Kitty.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPa capab trap\xc3\xa9 Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette, vouzot; mo p\xc3\xa9 couri vite cot\xc3\xa9 Miss Kitty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe called up to a mocking-bird warbling upon a lofty limb of a pine\r\ntree, asking why it cried out so, and threatening to secure it and put\r\nit into a cage. \xe2\x80\x9cCa to p\xc3\xa9 cri\xc3\xa9 comme \xc3\xa7a, ti c\xc3\xa9l\xc3\xa9ra? Arete, mo trap\xc3\xa9\r\nzozos la, mo m\xc3\xa9t\xc3\xa9 li dan ain bon lacage.\xe2\x80\x9d Indeed, Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette\r\nseemed on very familiar terms with the night, with the forest, and with\r\nall the flying, creeping, crawling things that inhabit it. At the speed\r\nwith which she traveled she soon had covered the few miles of wooded\r\nroad, and before long had reached her destination.\r\n\r\nThe sleeping-room of Miss Kitty opened upon the long outside gallery, as\r\ndid all the rooms of the unpretentious frame house which was her home.\r\nThe place could hardly be called a plantation; it was too small for\r\nthat. Nevertheless Raymond was trying to plant; trying to teach school\r\nbetween times, in the end room; and sometimes, when he found himself in\r\na tight place, trying to clerk for Mr. Jacobs over in Campte, across Red\r\nRiver.\r\n\r\nTante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette mounted the creaking steps, crossed the gallery, and\r\nentered Miss Kitty\xe2\x80\x99s room as though she were returning to it after a few\r\nmoments\xe2\x80\x99 absence. There was a lamp burning dimly upon the high\r\nmantelpiece. Raymond had evidently not been to bed; he was in shirt\r\nsleeves, rocking the baby\xe2\x80\x99s cradle. It was the same mahogany cradle\r\nwhich had held Miss Kitty thirty-five years before, when Tante\r\nCat\xe2\x80\x99rinette had rocked it. The cradle had been bought then to match the\r\nbed,\xe2\x80\x94that big, beautiful bed on which Miss Kitty lay now in a restless\r\nhalf slumber. There was a fine French clock on the mantel, still telling\r\nthe hours as it had told them years ago. But there were no carpets or\r\nrugs on the floors. There was no servant in the house.\r\n\r\nRaymond uttered an exclamation of amazement when he saw Tante\r\nCat\xe2\x80\x99rinette enter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow you do, Mich\xc3\xa9 Raymond?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cI yeard Miss Kitty been\r\nsick; Eus\xc3\xa8be tell me dat dis mo\xe2\x80\x99nin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe moved toward the bed as lightly as though shod with velvet, and\r\nseated herself there. Miss Kitty\xe2\x80\x99s hand lay outside the coverlid; a\r\nshapely hand, which her few days of illness and rest had not yet\r\nsoftened. The negress laid her own black hand upon it. At the touch Miss\r\nKitty instinctively turned her palm upward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, with a note of satisfaction in\r\nher feeble voice. \xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99en did you come, Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette? They all said\r\nyou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m goin\xe2\x80\x99 come ev\xe2\x80\x99y night, cher coeur, ev\xe2\x80\x99y night tell you be well.\r\nTante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette can\xe2\x80\x99t come daytime no mo\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRaymond tole me about it. They doin\xe2\x80\x99 you mighty mean in town, Tante\r\nCat\xe2\x80\x99rinette.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNev\xe2\x80\x99 mine, ti chou. I know how take care dat w\xe2\x80\x99at Vieumaite gi\xe2\x80\x99 me. You\r\ngo sleep now. Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette goin\xe2\x80\x99 set yere an\xe2\x80\x99 mine you. She goin\xe2\x80\x99 make\r\nyou well like she all time do. We don\xe2\x80\x99 wan\xe2\x80\x99 no c\xc3\xa9l\xc3\xa9ra doctor. We drive\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em out wid a stick, dey come roun\xe2\x80\x99 yere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Kitty was soon sleeping more restfully than she had done since her\r\nillness began. Raymond had finally succeeded in quieting the baby, and\r\nhe tiptoed into the adjoining room, where the other children lay, to\r\nsnatch a few hours of much-needed rest for himself. Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette sat\r\nfaithfully beside her charge, administering at intervals to the sick\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s wants.\r\n\r\nBut the thought of regaining her home before daybreak, and of the urgent\r\nnecessity for doing so, did not leave Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette\xe2\x80\x99s mind for an\r\ninstant.\r\n\r\nIn the profound darkness, the deep stillness of the night that comes\r\nbefore dawn, she was walking again through the woods, on her way back to\r\ntown.\r\n\r\nThe mocking-birds were asleep, and so were the frogs and the snakes; and\r\nthe moon was gone, and so was the breeze. She walked now in utter\r\nsilence but for the heavy guttural breathing that accompanied her rapid\r\nfootsteps. She walked with a desperate determination along the road,\r\nevery foot of which was familiar to her.\r\n\r\nWhen she at last emerged from the woods, the earth about her was\r\nfaintly, very faintly, beginning to reveal itself in the tremulous,\r\ngray, uncertain light of approaching day. She staggered and plunged\r\nonward with beating pulses quickened by fear.\r\n\r\nA sudden turn, and Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette stood facing the river. She stopped\r\nabruptly, as if at command of some unseen power that forced her. For an\r\ninstant she pressed a black hand against her tired, burning eyes, and\r\nstared fixedly ahead of her.\r\n\r\nTante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette had always believed that Paradise was up there\r\noverhead where the sun and stars and moon are, and that \xe2\x80\x9cVieumaite\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ninhabited that region of splendor. She never for a moment doubted this.\r\nIt would be difficult, perhaps unsatisfying, to explain why Tante\r\nCat\xe2\x80\x99rinette, on that particular morning, when a vision of the rising day\r\nbroke suddenly upon her, should have believed that she stood in face of\r\na heavenly revelation. But why not, after all? Since she talked so\r\nfamiliarly herself to the unseen, why should it not respond to her when\r\nthe time came?\r\n\r\nAcross the narrow, quivering line of water, the delicate budding\r\nbranches of young trees were limned black against the gold, orange,\xe2\x80\x94what\r\nword is there to tell the color of that morning sky! And steeped in the\r\nsplendor of it hung one pale star; there was not another in the whole\r\nheaven.\r\n\r\nTante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette stood with her eyes fixed intently upon that star,\r\nwhich held her like a hypnotic spell. She stammered breathlessly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMo p\xc3\xa9 cout\xc3\xa9, Vieumaite. Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette p\xc3\xa9 cout\xc3\xa9.\xe2\x80\x9d (I am listening,\r\nVieumaite. Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette hears you.)\r\n\r\nShe stayed there motionless upon the brink of the river till the star\r\nmelted into the brightness of the day and became part of it.\r\n\r\nWhen Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette entered Miss Kitty\xe2\x80\x99s room for the second time,\r\nthe aspect of things had changed somewhat. Miss Kitty was with much\r\ndifficulty holding the baby while Raymond mixed a saucer of food for the\r\nlittle one. Their oldest daughter, a child of twelve, had come into the\r\nroom with an apronful of chips from the woodpile, and was striving to\r\nstart a fire on the hearth, to make the morning coffee. The room seemed\r\nbare and almost squalid in the daylight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yere Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette come back,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, quietly announcing\r\nherself.\r\n\r\nThey could not well understand why she was back; but it was good to have\r\nher there, and they did not question.\r\n\r\nShe took the baby from its mother, and, seating herself, began to feed\r\nit from the saucer which Raymond placed beside her on a chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYas,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cCat\xe2\x80\x99rinette goin\xe2\x80\x99 stay; dis time she en\xe2\x80\x99t nev\xe2\x80\x99 goin\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x99way no mo\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHusband and wife looked at each other with surprised, questioning eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMich\xc3\xa9 Raymond,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the woman, turning her head up to him with a\r\ncertain comical shrewdness in her glance, \xe2\x80\x9cif somebody want len\xe2\x80\x99 you\r\nt\xe2\x80\x99ousan\xe2\x80\x99 dolla\xe2\x80\x99, w\xe2\x80\x99at you goin\xe2\x80\x99 say? Even if it\xe2\x80\x99s ole nigga \xe2\x80\x99oman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man\xe2\x80\x99s face flushed with sudden emotion. \xe2\x80\x9cI would say that person was\r\nour bes\xe2\x80\x99 frien\xe2\x80\x99, Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette. An\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, with a smile, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwould give her a mortgage on the place, of co\xe2\x80\x99se, to secu\xe2\x80\x99 her f\xe2\x80\x99om\r\nloss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDas right,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed the woman practically. \xe2\x80\x9cDen Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette goin\xe2\x80\x99 len\xe2\x80\x99\r\nyou t\xe2\x80\x99ousan\xe2\x80\x99 dolla\xe2\x80\x99. Dat w\xe2\x80\x99at Vieumaite give her, dat b\xe2\x80\x99long to her;\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99 b\xe2\x80\x99long to nobody else. An\xe2\x80\x99 we go yon\xe2\x80\x99a to town, Mich\xc3\xa9 Raymond, you\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 me. You care me befo\xe2\x80\x99 Mich\xc3\xa9 Paxtone. I want \xe2\x80\x99im fo\xe2\x80\x99 put down in\r\nwritin\xe2\x80\x99 befo\xe2\x80\x99 de cote dat w\xe2\x80\x99at Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette got, it fo\xe2\x80\x99 Miss Kitty w\xe2\x80\x99en I\r\nbe dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Kitty was crying softly in the depths of her pillow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI en\xe2\x80\x99t got no head fo\xe2\x80\x99 all dat, me,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Tante Cat\xe2\x80\x99rinette, good\r\nhumoredly, as she held a spoonful of pap up to the baby\xe2\x80\x99s eager lips.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Vieumaite tell me all dat clair an\xe2\x80\x99 plain dis mo\xe2\x80\x99nin\xe2\x80\x99, w\xe2\x80\x99en I\r\ncomin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99long de Gran\xe2\x80\x99 Eco\xe2\x80\x99 road.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n------------------------------------------------------------------------\r\n\r\n                          A Respectable Woman\r\n\r\n------------------------------------------------------------------------\r\n\r\n                          A Respectable Woman\r\n\r\n[Illustration]\r\n\r\n\r\nMrs. Baroda was a little provoked to learn that her husband expected his\r\nfriend, Gouvernail, up to spend a week or two on the plantation.\r\n\r\nThey had entertained a good deal during the winter; much of the time had\r\nalso been passed in New Orleans in various forms of mild dissipation.\r\nShe was looking forward to a period of unbroken rest, now, and\r\nundisturbed t\xc3\xaate-a-t\xc3\xaate with her husband, when he informed her that\r\nGouvernail was coming up to stay a week or two.\r\n\r\nThis was a man she had heard much of but never seen. He had been her\r\nhusband\xe2\x80\x99s college friend; was now a journalist, and in no sense a\r\nsociety man or \xe2\x80\x9ca man about town,\xe2\x80\x9d which were, perhaps, some of the\r\nreasons she had never met him. But she had unconsciously formed an image\r\nof him in her mind. She pictured him tall, slim, cynical; with\r\neye-glasses, and his hands in his pockets; and she did not like him.\r\nGouvernail was slim enough, but he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t very tall nor very cynical;\r\nneither did he wear eye-glasses nor carry his hands in his pockets. And\r\nshe rather liked him when he first presented himself.\r\n\r\nBut why she liked him she could not explain satisfactorily to herself\r\nwhen she partly attempted to do so. She could discover in him none of\r\nthose brilliant and promising traits which Gaston, her husband, had\r\noften assured her that he possessed. On the contrary, he sat rather mute\r\nand receptive before her chatty eagerness to make him feel at home and\r\nin face of Gaston\xe2\x80\x99s frank and wordy hospitality. His manner was as\r\ncourteous toward her as the most exacting woman could require; but he\r\nmade no direct appeal to her approval or even esteem.\r\n\r\nOnce settled at the plantation he seemed to like to sit upon the wide\r\nportico in the shade of one of the big Corinthian pillars, smoking his\r\ncigar lazily and listening attentively to Gaston\xe2\x80\x99s experience as a sugar\r\nplanter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is what I call living,\xe2\x80\x9d he would utter with deep satisfaction, as\r\nthe air that swept across the sugar field caressed him with its warm and\r\nscented velvety touch. It pleased him also to get on familiar terms with\r\nthe big dogs that came about him, rubbing themselves sociably against\r\nhis legs. He did not care to fish, and displayed no eagerness to go out\r\nand kill grosbecs when Gaston proposed doing so.\r\n\r\nGouvernail\xe2\x80\x99s personality puzzled Mrs. Baroda, but she liked him. Indeed,\r\nhe was a lovable, inoffensive fellow. After a few days, when she could\r\nunderstand him no better than at first, she gave over being puzzled and\r\nremained piqued. In this mood she left her husband and her guest, for\r\nthe most part, alone together. Then finding that Gouvernail took no\r\nmanner of exception to her action, she imposed her society upon him,\r\naccompanying him in his idle strolls to the mill and walks along the\r\nbatture. She persistently sought to penetrate the reserve in which he\r\nhad unconsciously enveloped himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen is he going\xe2\x80\x94your friend?\xe2\x80\x9d she one day asked her husband. \xe2\x80\x9cFor my\r\npart, he tires me frightfully.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for a week yet, dear. I can\xe2\x80\x99t understand; he gives you no trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I should like him better if he did; if he were more like others,\r\nand I had to plan somewhat for his comfort and enjoyment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGaston took his wife\xe2\x80\x99s pretty face between his hands and looked tenderly\r\nand laughingly into her troubled eyes. They were making a bit of toilet\r\nsociably together in Mrs. Baroda\xe2\x80\x99s dressing-room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are full of surprises, ma belle,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to her. \xe2\x80\x9cEven I can never\r\ncount upon how you are going to act under given conditions.\xe2\x80\x9d He kissed\r\nher and turned to fasten his cravat before the mirror.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere you are,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9ctaking poor Gouvernail seriously and making\r\na commotion over him, the last thing he would desire or expect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCommotion!\xe2\x80\x9d she hotly resented. \xe2\x80\x9cNonsense! How can you say such a\r\nthing? Commotion, indeed! But, you know, you said he was clever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo he is. But the poor fellow is run down by overwork now. That\xe2\x80\x99s why I\r\nasked him here to take a rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou used to say he was a man of ideas,\xe2\x80\x9d she retorted, unconciliated. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nexpected him to be interesting, at least. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to the city in the\r\nmorning to have my spring gowns fitted. Let me know when Mr. Gouvernail\r\nis gone; I shall be at my Aunt Octavie\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat night she went and sat alone upon a bench that stood beneath a live\r\noak tree at the edge of the gravel walk.\r\n\r\nShe had never known her thoughts or her intentions to be so confused.\r\nShe could gather nothing from them but the feeling of a distinct\r\nnecessity to quit her home in the morning.\r\n\r\nMrs. Baroda heard footsteps crunching the gravel; but could discern in\r\nthe darkness only the approaching red point of a lighted cigar. She knew\r\nit was Gouvernail, for her husband did not smoke. She hoped to remain\r\nunnoticed, but her white gown revealed her to him. He threw away his\r\ncigar and seated himself upon the bench beside her; without a suspicion\r\nthat she might object to his presence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour husband told me to bring this to you, Mrs. Baroda,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\nhanding her a filmy, white scarf with which she sometimes enveloped her\r\nhead and shoulders. She accepted the scarf from him with a murmur of\r\nthanks, and let it lie in her lap.\r\n\r\nHe made some commonplace observation upon the baneful effect of the\r\nnight air at that season. Then as his gaze reached out into the\r\ndarkness, he murmured, half to himself:\r\n\r\n          \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Night of south winds\xe2\x80\x94night of the large few stars!\r\n            Still nodding night\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe made no reply to this apostrophe to the night, which indeed, was not\r\naddressed to her.\r\n\r\nGouvernail was in no sense a diffident man, for he was not a\r\nself-conscious one. His periods of reserve were not constitutional, but\r\nthe result of moods. Sitting there beside Mrs. Baroda, his silence\r\nmelted for the time.\r\n\r\nHe talked freely and intimately in a low, hesitating drawl that was not\r\nunpleasant to hear. He talked of the old college days when he and Gaston\r\nhad been a good deal to each other; of the days of keen and blind\r\nambitions and large intentions. Now there was left with him, at least, a\r\nphilosophic acquiescence to the existing order\xe2\x80\x94only a desire to be\r\npermitted to exist, with now and then a little whiff of genuine life,\r\nsuch as he was breathing now.\r\n\r\nHer mind only vaguely grasped what he was saying. Her physical being was\r\nfor the moment predominant. She was not thinking of his words, only\r\ndrinking in the tones of his voice. She wanted to reach out her hand in\r\nthe darkness and touch him with the sensitive tips of her fingers upon\r\nthe face or the lips. She wanted to draw close to him and whisper\r\nagainst his cheek\xe2\x80\x94she did not care what\xe2\x80\x94as she might have done if she\r\nhad not been a respectable woman.\r\n\r\nThe stronger the impulse grew to bring herself near him, the further, in\r\nfact, did she draw away from him. As soon as she could do so without an\r\nappearance of too great rudeness, she rose and left him there alone.\r\n\r\nBefore she reached the house, Gouvernail had lighted a fresh cigar and\r\nended his apostrophe to the night.\r\n\r\nMrs. Baroda was greatly tempted that night to tell her husband\xe2\x80\x94who was\r\nalso her friend\xe2\x80\x94of this folly that had seized her. But she did not yield\r\nto the temptation. Beside being a respectable woman she was a very\r\nsensible one; and she knew there are some battles in life which a human\r\nbeing must fight alone.\r\n\r\nWhen Gaston arose in the morning, his wife had already departed. She had\r\ntaken an early morning train to the city. She did not return till\r\nGouvernail was gone from under her roof.\r\n\r\nThere was some talk of having him back during the summer that followed.\r\nThat is, Gaston greatly desired it; but this desire yielded to his\r\nwife\xe2\x80\x99s strenuous opposition.\r\n\r\nHowever, before the year ended, she proposed, wholly from herself, to\r\nhave Gouvernail visit them again. Her husband was surprised and\r\ndelighted with the suggestion coming from her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad, ch\xc3\xa8re amie, to know that you have finally overcome your\r\ndislike for him; truly he did not deserve it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d she told him, laughingly, after pressing a long, tender kiss upon\r\nhis lips, \xe2\x80\x9cI have overcome everything! you will see. This time I shall\r\nbe very nice to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n------------------------------------------------------------------------\r\n\r\n                               Ripe Figs\r\n\r\n------------------------------------------------------------------------\r\n\r\n                               Ripe Figs\r\n\r\n[Illustration]\r\n\r\n\r\nMaman-Nainaine said that when the figs were ripe Babette might go to\r\nvisit her cousins down on the Bayou-Lafourche where the sugar cane\r\ngrows. Not that the ripening of figs had the least thing to do with it,\r\nbut that is the way Maman-Nainaine was.\r\n\r\nIt seemed to Babette a very long time to wait; for the leaves upon the\r\ntrees were tender yet, and the figs were like little hard, green\r\nmarbles.\r\n\r\nBut warm rains came along and plenty of strong sunshine, and though\r\nMaman-Nainaine was as patient as the statue of la Madone, and Babette as\r\nrestless as a humming-bird, the first thing they both knew it was hot\r\nsummer-time. Every day Babette danced out to where the fig-trees were in\r\na long line against the fence. She walked slowly beneath them, carefully\r\npeering between the gnarled, spreading branches. But each time she came\r\ndisconsolate away again. What she saw there finally was something that\r\nmade her sing and dance the whole long day.\r\n\r\nWhen Maman-Nainaine sat down in her stately way to breakfast, the\r\nfollowing morning, her muslin cap standing like an aureole about her\r\nwhite, placid face, Babette approached. She bore a dainty porcelain\r\nplatter, which she set down before her godmother. It contained a dozen\r\npurple figs, fringed around with their rich, green leaves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maman-Nainaine, arching her eyebrows, \xe2\x80\x9chow early the figs\r\nhave ripened this year!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Babette, \xe2\x80\x9cI think they have ripened very late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBabette,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Maman-Nainaine, as she peeled the very plumpest\r\nfigs with her pointed silver fruit-knife, \xe2\x80\x9cyou will carry my love to\r\nthem all down on Bayou-Lafourche. And tell your Tante Frosine I shall\r\nlook for her at Toussaint\xe2\x80\x94when the chrysanthemums are in bloom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n------------------------------------------------------------------------\r\n\r\n                            Oz\xc3\xa8me\xe2\x80\x99s Holiday\r\n\r\n------------------------------------------------------------------------\r\n\r\n                            Oz\xc3\xa8me\xe2\x80\x99s Holiday\r\n\r\n[Illustration]\r\n\r\n\r\nOz\xc3\xa8me often wondered why there was not a special dispensation of\r\nprovidence to do away with the necessity for work. There seemed to him\r\nso much created for man\xe2\x80\x99s enjoyment in this world, and so little time\r\nand opportunity to profit by it. To sit and do nothing but breathe was a\r\npleasure to Oz\xc3\xa8me; but to sit in the company of a few choice companions,\r\nincluding a sprinkling of ladies, was even a greater delight; and the\r\njoy which a day\xe2\x80\x99s hunting or fishing or picnicking afforded him is\r\nhardly to be described. Yet he was by no means indolent. He worked\r\nfaithfully on the plantation the whole year long, in a sort of\r\nmethodical way; but when the time came around for his annual week\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nholiday, there was no holding him back. It was often decidedly\r\ninconvenient for the planter that Oz\xc3\xa8me usually chose to take his\r\nholiday during some very busy season of the year.\r\n\r\nHe started out one morning in the beginning of October. He had borrowed\r\nMr. Laballi\xc3\xa8re\xe2\x80\x99s buckboard and Padue\xe2\x80\x99s old gray mare, and a harness from\r\nthe negro S\xc3\xa9v\xc3\xa9rin. He wore a light blue suit which had been sent all the\r\nway from St. Louis, and which had cost him ten dollars; he had paid\r\nalmost as much again for his boots; and his hat was a broad-rimmed gray\r\nfelt which he had no cause to be ashamed of. When Oz\xc3\xa8me went \xe2\x80\x9cbroading,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe dressed\xe2\x80\x94well, regardless of cost. His eyes were blue and mild; his\r\nhair was light, and he wore it rather long; he was clean shaven, and\r\nreally did not look his thirty-five years.\r\n\r\nOz\xc3\xa8me had laid his plans weeks beforehand. He was going visiting along\r\nCane River; the mere contemplation filled him with pleasure. He counted\r\nupon reaching F\xc3\xa9deaus\xe2\x80\x99 about noon, and he would stop and dine there.\r\nPerhaps they would ask him to stay all night. He really did not hold to\r\nstaying all night, and was not decided to accept if they did ask him.\r\nThere were only the two old people, and he rather fancied the notion of\r\npushing on to Beltrans\xe2\x80\x99, where he would stay a night, or even two, if\r\nurged. He was quite sure that there would be something agreeable going\r\non at Beltrans\xe2\x80\x99, with all those young people\xe2\x80\x94perhaps a fish-fry, or\r\npossibly a ball!\r\n\r\nOf course he would have to give a day to Tante Sophie and another to\r\nCousine Victoire; but none to the St. Annes unless entreated\xe2\x80\x94after St.\r\nAnne reproaching him last year with being a fain\xc3\xa9ant for broading at\r\nsuch a season! At Cloutierville, where he would linger as long as\r\npossible, he meant to turn and retrace his course, zigzagging back and\r\nforth across Cane River so as to take in the Duplans, the Velcours, and\r\nothers that he could not at the moment recall. A week seemed to Oz\xc3\xa8me a\r\nvery, very little while in which to crowd so much pleasure.\r\n\r\nThere were steam-gins at work; he could hear them whistling far and\r\nnear. On both sides of the river the fields were white with cotton, and\r\neverybody in the world seemed busy but Oz\xc3\xa8me. This reflection did not\r\ndistress or disturb him in the least; he pursued his way at peace with\r\nhimself and his surroundings.\r\n\r\nAt Lam\xc3\xa9rie\xe2\x80\x99s cross-roads store, where he stopped to buy a cigar, he\r\nlearned that there was no use heading for F\xc3\xa9deaus\xe2\x80\x99, as the two old\r\npeople had gone to town for a lengthy visit, and the house was locked\r\nup. It was at F\xc3\xa9deaus\xe2\x80\x99 that Oz\xc3\xa8me had intended to dine.\r\n\r\nHe sat in the buckboard, given up to a moment or two of reflection. The\r\nresult was that he turned away from the river, and entered the road that\r\nled between two fields back to the woods and into the heart of the\r\ncountry. He had determined upon taking a short cut to the Beltrans\xe2\x80\x99\r\nplantation, and on the way he meant to keep an eye open for old Aunt\r\nTildy\xe2\x80\x99s cabin, which he knew lay in some remote part of this cut-off. He\r\nremembered that Aunt Tildy could cook an excellent meal if she had the\r\nmaterial at hand. He would induce her to fry him a chicken, drip a cup\r\nof coffee, and turn him out a pone of corn-bread, which he thought would\r\nbe sumptuous enough fare for the occasion.\r\n\r\nAunt Tildy dwelt in the not unusual log cabin, of one room, with its\r\nchimney of mud and stone, and its shallow gallery formed by the jutting\r\nof the roof. In close proximity to the cabin was a small cotton-field,\r\nwhich from a long distance looked like a field of snow. The cotton was\r\nbursting and overflowing foam-like from bolls on the drying stalk. On\r\nthe lower branches it was hanging ragged and tattered, and much of it\r\nhad already fallen to the ground. There were a few chinaberry-trees in\r\nthe yard before the hut, and under one of them an ancient and\r\nrusty-looking mule was eating corn from a wood trough. Some common\r\nlittle Creole chickens were scratching about the mule\xe2\x80\x99s feet and\r\nsnatching at the grains of corn that occasionally fell from the trough.\r\n\r\nAunt Tildy was hobbling across the yard when Oz\xc3\xa8me drew up before the\r\ngate. One hand was confined in a sling; in the other she carried a tin\r\npan, which she let fall noisily to the ground when she recognized him.\r\nShe was broad, black, and misshapen, with her body bent forward almost\r\nat an acute angle. She wore a blue cottonade of large plaids, and a\r\nbandana awkwardly twisted around her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood God A\xe2\x80\x99mighty, man! Whar you come from?\xe2\x80\x9d was her startled\r\nexclamation at beholding him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cF\xe2\x80\x99om home, Aunt Tildy; w\xe2\x80\x99ere else do you expec\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d replied Oz\xc3\xa8me,\r\ndismounting composedly.\r\n\r\nHe had not seen the old woman for several years\xe2\x80\x94since she was cooking in\r\ntown for the family with which he boarded at the time. She had washed\r\nand ironed for him, atrociously, it is true, but her intentions were\r\nbeyond reproach if her washing was not. She had also been clumsily\r\nattentive to him during a spell of illness. He had paid her with an\r\noccasional bandana, a calico dress, or a checked apron, and they had\r\nalways considered the account between themselves square, with no\r\nsentimental feeling of gratitude remaining on either side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like to know,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Oz\xc3\xa8me, as he took the gray mare from the\r\nshafts, and led her up to the trough where the mule was\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI like to know\r\nw\xe2\x80\x99at you mean by makin\xe2\x80\x99 a crop like that an\xe2\x80\x99 then lettin\xe2\x80\x99 it go to\r\nwas\xe2\x80\x99e? Who you reckon\xe2\x80\x99s goin\xe2\x80\x99 to pick that cotton? You think maybe the\r\nangels goin\xe2\x80\x99 to come down an\xe2\x80\x99 pick it fo\xe2\x80\x99 you, an\xe2\x80\x99 gin it an\xe2\x80\x99 press it,\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 then give you ten cents a poun\xe2\x80\x99 fo\xe2\x80\x99 it, hein?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEf de Lord don\xe2\x80\x99 pick it, I don\xe2\x80\x99 know who gwine pick it, Mista Oz\xc3\xa8me. I\r\ntell you, me an\xe2\x80\x99 Sandy we wuk dat crap day in an\xe2\x80\x99 day out; it\xe2\x80\x99s him done\r\nde mos\xe2\x80\x99 of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSandy? That little\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe ain\xe2\x80\x99 dat li\xe2\x80\x99le Sandy no mo\xe2\x80\x99 w\xe2\x80\x99at you rec\xe2\x80\x99lec\xe2\x80\x99s; he \xe2\x80\x99mos\xe2\x80\x99 a man, an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhe wuk like a man now. He wuk mo\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99an fittin\xe2\x80\x99 fo\xe2\x80\x99 his strenk, an\xe2\x80\x99 now he\r\nlayin\xe2\x80\x99 in dah sick\xe2\x80\x94God A\xe2\x80\x99mighty knows how sick. An\xe2\x80\x99 me wid a risin\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntwell I bleeged to walk de flo\xe2\x80\x99 o\xe2\x80\x99 nights, an\xe2\x80\x99 don\xe2\x80\x99 know ef I ain\xe2\x80\x99 gwine\r\nto lose de han\xe2\x80\x99 atter all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99y, in the name o\xe2\x80\x99 conscience, you don\xe2\x80\x99 hire somebody to pick?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhar I got money to hire? An\xe2\x80\x99 you knows well as me ev\xe2\x80\x99y chick an\xe2\x80\x99 chile\r\nis pickin\xe2\x80\x99 roun\xe2\x80\x99 on de plantations an\xe2\x80\x99 gittin\xe2\x80\x99 good pay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe whole outlook appeared to Oz\xc3\xa8me very depressing, and even menacing,\r\nto his personal comfort and peace of mind. He foresaw no prospect of\r\ndinner unless he should cook it himself. And there was that Sandy\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nremembered well the little scamp of eight, always at his grandmother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheels when she was cooking or washing. Of course he would have to go in\r\nand look at the boy, and no doubt dive into his traveling-bag for\r\nquinine, without which he never traveled.\r\n\r\nSandy was indeed very ill, consumed with fever. He lay on a cot covered\r\nup with a faded patchwork quilt. His eyes were half closed, and he was\r\nmuttering and rambling on about hoeing and bedding and cleaning and\r\nthinning out the cotton; he was hauling it to the gin, wrangling about\r\nweight and bagging and ties and the price offered per pound. That bale\r\nor two of cotton had not only sent Sandy to bed, but had pursued him\r\nthere, holding him through his fevered dreams, and threatening to end\r\nhim. Oz\xc3\xa8me would never have known the black boy, he was so tall, so\r\nthin, and seemingly so wasted, lying there in bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee yere, Aunt Tildy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oz\xc3\xa8me, after he had, as was usual with him\r\nwhen in doubt, abandoned himself to a little reflection; \xe2\x80\x9cbetween us\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 me\xe2\x80\x94we got to manage to kill an\xe2\x80\x99 cook one o\xe2\x80\x99 those chickens I see\r\nscratchin\xe2\x80\x99 out yonda, fo\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99m jus\xe2\x80\x99 about starved. I reckon you ain\xe2\x80\x99t got\r\nany quinine in the house? No; I didn\xe2\x80\x99t suppose an instant you had. Well,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m goin\xe2\x80\x99 to give Sandy a good dose o\xe2\x80\x99 quinine to-night, an\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99m goin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nstay an\xe2\x80\x99 see how that\xe2\x80\x99ll work on \xe2\x80\x99im. But sun-up, min\xe2\x80\x99 you, I mus\xe2\x80\x99 get\r\nout o\xe2\x80\x99 yere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOz\xc3\xa8me had spent more comfortable nights than the one passed in Aunt\r\nTildy\xe2\x80\x99s bed, which she considerately abandoned to him.\r\n\r\nIn the morning Sandy\xe2\x80\x99s fever was somewhat abated, but had not taken a\r\ndecided enough turn to justify Oz\xc3\xa8me in quitting him before noon, unless\r\nhe was willing \xe2\x80\x9cto feel like a dog,\xe2\x80\x9d as he told himself. He appeared\r\nbefore Aunt Tildy stripped to the undershirt, and wearing his\r\nsecond-best pair of trousers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a nice pickle o\xe2\x80\x99 fish you got me in, ol\xe2\x80\x99 woman. I guarantee,\r\nnex\xe2\x80\x99 time I go abroad, \xe2\x80\x99tain\xe2\x80\x99t me that\xe2\x80\x99ll take any cut-off. W\xe2\x80\x99ere\xe2\x80\x99s that\r\ncotton-basket an\xe2\x80\x99 cotton-sack o\xe2\x80\x99 yo\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knowed it!\xe2\x80\x9d chanted Aunt Tildy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI knowed de Lord war gwine sen\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsomebody to holp me out. He war n\xe2\x80\x99 gwine let de crap was\xe2\x80\x99e atter he give\r\nSandy an\xe2\x80\x99 me de strenk to make hit. De Lord gwine shove you \xe2\x80\x99long de\r\nrow, Mista Oz\xc3\xa8me. De Lord gwine give you plenty mo\xe2\x80\x99 fingers an\xe2\x80\x99 han\xe2\x80\x99s to\r\npick dat cotton nimble an\xe2\x80\x99 clean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeva you min\xe2\x80\x99 w\xe2\x80\x99at the Lord\xe2\x80\x99s goin\xe2\x80\x99 to do; go get me that cotton-sack.\r\nAn\xe2\x80\x99 you put that poultice like I tol\xe2\x80\x99 you on yo\xe2\x80\x99 han\xe2\x80\x99, an\xe2\x80\x99 set down\r\nthere an\xe2\x80\x99 watch Sandy. It looks like you are \xe2\x80\x99bout as helpless as a\xe2\x80\x99 ol\xe2\x80\x99\r\ncow tangled up in a potato-vine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOz\xc3\xa8me had not picked cotton for many years, and he took to it a little\r\nawkwardly at first; but by the time he had reached the end of the first\r\nrow the old dexterity of youth had come back to his hands, which flew\r\nrapidly back and forth with the motion of a weaver\xe2\x80\x99s shuttle; and his\r\nten fingers became really nimble in clutching the cotton from its dry\r\nshell. By noon he had gathered about fifty pounds. Sandy was not then\r\nquite so well as he had promised to be, and Oz\xc3\xa8me concluded to stay that\r\nday and one more night. If the boy were no better in the morning, he\r\nwould go off in search of a doctor for him, and he himself would\r\ncontinue on down to Tante Sophie\xe2\x80\x99s; the Beltrans\xe2\x80\x99 was out of the\r\nquestion now.\r\n\r\nSandy hardly needed a doctor in the morning. Oz\xc3\xa8me\xe2\x80\x99s doctoring was\r\nbeginning to tell favorably; but he would have considered it criminal\r\nindifference and negligence to go away and leave the boy to Aunt Tildy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nawkward ministrations just at the critical moment when there was a turn\r\nfor the better; so he stayed that day out, and picked his hundred and\r\nfifty pounds.\r\n\r\nOn the third day it looked like rain, and a heavy rain just then would\r\nmean a heavy loss to Aunt Tildy and Sandy, and Oz\xc3\xa8me again went to the\r\nfield, this time urging Aunt Tildy with him to do what she might with\r\nher one good hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Tildy,\xe2\x80\x9d called out Oz\xc3\xa8me to the bent old woman moving ahead of him\r\nbetween the white rows of cotton, \xe2\x80\x9cif the Lord gets me safe out o\xe2\x80\x99 this\r\nditch, \xe2\x80\x99t ain\xe2\x80\x99t to-morro\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99ll fall in anotha with my eyes open, I bet\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep along, Mista Oz\xc3\xa8me; don\xe2\x80\x99 grumble, don\xe2\x80\x99 stumble; de Lord\xe2\x80\x99s\r\na-watchin\xe2\x80\x99 you. Look at yo\xe2\x80\x99 Aunt Tildy; she doin\xe2\x80\x99 mo\xe2\x80\x99 wid her one han\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x99an you doin\xe2\x80\x99 wid yo\xe2\x80\x99 two, man. Keep right along, honey. Watch dat\r\ncotton how it fallin\xe2\x80\x99 in yo\xe2\x80\x99 Aunt Tildy\xe2\x80\x99s bag.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am watchin\xe2\x80\x99 you, ol\xe2\x80\x99 woman; you don\xe2\x80\x99 fool me. You got to work that\r\nhan\xe2\x80\x99 o\xe2\x80\x99 yo\xe2\x80\x99s spryer than you doin\xe2\x80\x99, or I\xe2\x80\x99ll take the rawhide. You done\r\nfo\xe2\x80\x99got w\xe2\x80\x99at the rawhide tas\xe2\x80\x99e like, I reckon\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94a reminder which amused\r\nAunt Tildy so powerfully that her big negro-laugh resounded over the\r\nwhole cotton-patch, and even caused Sandy, who heard it, to turn in his\r\nbed.\r\n\r\nThe weather was still threatening on the succeeding day, and a sort of\r\ndogged determination or characteristic desire to see his undertakings\r\ncarried to a satisfactory completion urged Oz\xc3\xa8me to continue his efforts\r\nto drag Aunt Tildy out of the mire into which circumstances seemed to\r\nhave thrust her.\r\n\r\nOne night the rain did come, and began to beat softly on the roof of the\r\nold cabin. Sandy opened his eyes, which were no longer brilliant with\r\nthe fever flame. \xe2\x80\x9cGranny,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cde rain! Des listen, granny;\r\nde rain a-comin\xe2\x80\x99, an\xe2\x80\x99 I ain\xe2\x80\x99 pick dat cotton yit. W\xe2\x80\x99at time it is? Gi\xe2\x80\x99\r\nme my pants\xe2\x80\x94I got to go\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou lay whar you is, chile alive. Dat cotton put aside clean and dry.\r\nMe an\xe2\x80\x99 de Lord an\xe2\x80\x99 Mista Oz\xc3\xa8me done pick dat cotton.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOz\xc3\xa8me drove away in the morning looking quite as spick and span as the\r\nday he left home in his blue suit and his light felt drawn a little over\r\nhis eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou want to take care o\xe2\x80\x99 that boy,\xe2\x80\x9d he instructed Aunt Tildy at\r\nparting, \xe2\x80\x9can\xe2\x80\x99 get \xe2\x80\x99im on his feet. An\xe2\x80\x99, let me tell you, the nex\xe2\x80\x99 time I\r\nstart out to broad, if you see me passin\xe2\x80\x99 in this yere cut-off, put on\r\nyo\xe2\x80\x99 specs an\xe2\x80\x99 look at me good, because it won\xe2\x80\x99t be me; it\xe2\x80\x99ll be my\r\nghos\xe2\x80\x99, ol\xe2\x80\x99 woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIndeed, Oz\xc3\xa8me, for some reason or other, felt quite shamefaced as he\r\ndrove back to the plantation. When he emerged from the lane which he had\r\nentered the week before, and turned into the river road, Lam\xc3\xa9rie,\r\nstanding in the store door, shouted out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xc3\xa9, Oz\xc3\xa8me! you had good times yonda? I bet you danced holes in the sole\r\nof them new boots.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t talk, Lam\xc3\xa9rie!\xe2\x80\x9d was Oz\xc3\xa8me\xe2\x80\x99s rather ambiguous reply, as he\r\nflourished the remainder of a whip over the old gray mare\xe2\x80\x99s sway-back,\r\nurging her to a gentle trot.\r\n\r\nWhen he reached home, Bod\xc3\xa9, one of Padue\xe2\x80\x99s boys, who was assisting him\r\nto unhitch, remarked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow come you didn\xe2\x80\x99 go yonda down de coas\xe2\x80\x99 like you said, Mista Oz\xc3\xa8me?\r\nNobody didn\xe2\x80\x99 see you in Cloutierville, an\xe2\x80\x99 Mailitte say you neva cross\xe2\x80\x99\r\nde twenty-fo\xe2\x80\x99-mile ferry, an\xe2\x80\x99 nobody didn\xe2\x80\x99 see you no place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOz\xc3\xa8me returned, after his customary moment of reflection:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, it\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x99mos\xe2\x80\x99 always the same thing on Cane riva, my boy; a man\r\ngets tired o\xe2\x80\x99 that \xc3\xa0 la fin. This time I went back in the woods, \xe2\x80\x99way\r\nyonda in the F\xc3\xa9deau cut-off kin\xe2\x80\x99 o\xe2\x80\x99 campin\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 roughin\xe2\x80\x99 like, you might\r\nsay. I tell you, it was sport, Bod\xc3\xa9.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n     PRESS OF\r\nSTROMBERG, ALLEN & CO.\r\n     CHICAGO\r\n\r\n------------------------------------------------------------------------\r\n\r\n                           Transcriber\xe2\x80\x99s Note\r\n\r\nErrors deemed most likely to be the printer\xe2\x80\x99s have been corrected, and\r\nare noted here.
